Tales of the Early Days.
No. XIV.—The Paying-Out of Constable
Crake.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG
There is a great Australian family which
boasts a recently invented shield and crest. The
bearings are described in “Burke” with a rich
luxuriance of heraldic jargon. Truthfully, they
should be a woman, pendant, vert* on a gallows
sinister, sable. This is a story of the bravest deed
eVet done by a member of that family; which
deed being, what it was, is not borne from genera-
tion to generation on the perfumed breath of
tradition. The younger members are taught to
look with a reverence almost religions upon the
inch of ribbon and the fragment of parchment
which symbolise some ridiculous Imperial “honor,”
conferred for Heaven alone knows what upon one
of its later chieftains, but the deed which removes
their “colonial founders” from the ruck of
humanity they are never taught. The people who
should teach them are ashamed of it. Which is
human history. The great deeds of the world are
the Unhonoured ones,
# * *
Hyde Park Barracks, Sydney, in the
Early Twenties. The buildings stood then much
as they are to-day, where they form Chancery
Square. There is, however, a slight difference in
their tenants. Then, they consisted of persons
who had fallen beneath the lash of the law, Now,
they are the persons who wield that lash. The
difference between the respective classes of tenants
extend to their manners. Between the coarseness
of speech and gesture of the convicts and the
polished blackguardism of the present-day fol-
lowers of the LaW there lies the chasm created by
two generations of culture, In morals, however,
there is no divergence of character.
A Wall, 10ft. Gin, high, separated at the
time of our story the barrack enclosure, on
the south and west, from the open, un-
fenced space called indiscriminately thp. Race-
course, the Parade-ground, and Hyde Bark;
on the north from the General Hospital en-
closure and the pleasure grounds of the Go-
vernor. The entrance was from the west, the
gates being guarded by two lodges 12 feet square
—the one on the right being appropriated to the
clerks, the other to those indispensable accessories
of the System, the “freed” constables. Other
offices and minor buildings formed then, as they
form now, a lining to the walls, clasping, as it
were, in their embrace, the principal building.
This was the main barrack. It deserves a niche of
distinction in architectural history, for it was the
first building, not in Australia alone, but in the
British Empire, arranged specifically for the
classification of prisoners according to the degrees
of their Criminality. Governor Macquarie, if for
no other reason, deserves to be honoured in that
he attempted to solve a problem which English-
speaking legislatures everywhere grow daily elo-
quent about while leaving unsolved. If delay
in the work of social reform be playing the game
of the devil, what a capable partner he has in the
English-speaking legislator!
The barrack was of three storeys. On each
floor there were four rooms. A passage, 12 feet
wide, ran the length of the building. Two rooms
—those facing west —were 65 feet long; the other
two were 35 feet long. The breadth of each room
was 19 feet.
In each of the six long rooms 70 men slept. In
each of the six small ones, 35. As a rule, that is.
But one January night, in the Early Twenties,
No. 5 room, the small one on the east-end of the
upper floor, contained 33 men—and one girl. She
was the foundress of the great Australian family
aforesaid.
* * #
The grass-cutting gang had, with their cox-
swains, duly reported themselves about 5.30 in the
afternoon. They were rather later than usual,
but the wind had been blowing strongly against
the boats as they bore down heavily laden with
grass for the Government live-stock from the
Parramatta River, and such of their crews as
were not privileged to lodge in the town—that is
to say, some twenty-one or two out of the forty
who constituted the gang, were accordingly half-
an-hour “beyond time” in reporting at “night
muster.” But the System, surprising to say, had
not endeavoured to control the tides and winds —
had, indeed, made no provision for such an emer-
gency as had now risen, and did not, therefore,
punish the gang by stopping their rations. All it
did was to give them their food cold when the
mustering overseer had ended.
“ Cox’n Nicholson—crew right ?”
“Yes, sir! Arnold—Russell—Green—Dixon —
Hawkins—King !”
As he called his crew, the overseer ticked the
names off and ordered them to pass to mess.
“ Cox’n Wilmott —yours ?”
“Yes, sir!” And the like routine was
observed.
“ Cox’n Tribe —yours ?”
“Yes, sir! Hume —Evans—Grant, father —
Grant, son—Dillon —Lee!”
“Right! Pass on !”
“ Beg parding, sir—Rut I don’t think as Grant,
father, isn’t well, sir!” The crew, shuffling off,
paused to wait response to this reference to a fact
they had all been made aware of that day.
“Ain’t you well, Grant?” the overseer asked.
The old man, who as yet was not so old, was of
medium height, which appeared the less because
of the stoop that spoke of his use of the sickle as
a grass-cutting tool. His features were those of a
weakling, the cheeks had tumbled in and the chin
fell away to a point, in the fashion which, though
the man were dumb, would convey an accent of
servility. The fellow was a typical servant, with-
out a mind or a tongue ora backbone of his own.
When, in his free days, “ Master ” paid him his
wages, “ Master ” owned him body and soul. And
now the System owned him, and he was abjectly
servile to it. When interrogated, he did not know
whether lie would please or offend the Overseer by
telling the truth, although it was patent he was
ill. He was trembling ; “ Grant, son,” held him
up, otherwise he would have fallen.
“Are you ill, Grant? Can’t you answer?”
“Grant, father,” looked at his son. That was
his way always. It was “ Grant, son,” who ever
answered for the old man—did some of his work
for him—cared for him.
“ Yes, he’s very bad, Mr. Grove. I’m afraid
he’s had a stroke, sir.”
Grove, the Mustering-Overseer, turned to the
Coxswain.
“ Are you sure he ain’t a-shammin’, Tribe ? ”
“ No, sir—l mean, sir, as am sure he isn’t sham-
ming. I thought as ’twas a kin’ o’ stroke myself,
sir ! ”
“ Too ill to wait for mornin’ doctor ?”
“ Yess’r, I’m afraid, sir !”
“ What d’ you say, Grant, son? ”
“ I can’t make him out at all, sir—l never saw
him like this afore ! I’ll work double to-morrow,
sir, it you’ll let him go to the horspital to-night.”
“ Was he much short to-day ?”
The daily task of each man in a cutting-gang
was forty bundles, say a hundredweight of grass
per day. Barney Williams, Governor’s coxswain,
checked the quantity nightly.
Coxswain Tribe looked around at his men.
Should he tell? Grove seemed in a pretty good
temper to-night, but there was never any telling
how long a subordinate officer’s amiability would
last. Perhaps if he told the truth it would be all
right—perhaps all wrong. However, he risked it.
“He didn’t do nothing at all to-day, sir. The
coves all helped him, besides doin’ their own
whack,”
“ Were any of them short?”
“ No, sir; Mr. Williams passed the lot.”
“ All right, then. But no more oodgering o’
that sort is to be allowed. If the man’s sick, let
him report sick afore he goes. An’ if he goes, he’s
got to do his task. An’ if there’s time for the
others to do his job besides their own, there’s
time to do more for Government. We’ll see if the
grass-cutters can’t make up sixty bundles a-day,
instead of forty. An’ you’re sure Grant, father,
isn’t shamrain’ Abram, Tribe?”
“Yes, sir—quite.”
“Fall out Grant, father, for hospital—an’ pass
to mess the rest. March !”
The gangers saluted and passed to their mess-
room. This was a long room on the southern side
of the enclosure, the kitchen separating it from a
similar room where the “town gangs” messed.
And “Grant, father ” stood, or rather staggered,
solitary, save for the Overseer, in the mustering
space by the gateway. He looked dazedly after
the gangers, for with them went “Grant, son.”
The younger man would have stayed had he
dared, but there was never any certainty about
the System’s methods of interpretation A reluct-
ance to obey, which might have been prompted by
a pure filial regard, might have been construed as
insubordination. Had not a fellow been haled
from that very yard to the neighbouring triangle
—a statue of “Albert the Good” now ennobles
the ensanguined spot!—for marching to the dor-
mitory with his right foot first, when the order
had been given by the ex-soldier overseer to lead
off with the left? 8o “ Grant, son ’’—not “ Grant,
second,” that would have meant a man of the
same name, but no kinsman; nor “Grant, the
younger,” for that would have signified a younger
brother, whose elder was on the same muster-roll —
Grant, son, moved to his food. But it choked
him. For, almost for the first time since they had
been hustled from the hulk at Plymouth to the
convict-ship, father and son were separated. With
the ship’s indent had come a memorandum-order
signed by a Very Great Personage indeed, request-
ing Governor Macquarie to, as far as possible,
keep father and son together. And Mr. Secretary
Campbell, who since 1810 had personally super-
vised every “musteron arrival,” had ordered that
the two Grants should be placed in the same gang.
And Mr. Campbell was, to the official intellect, a
much greater man than the Personage, for he was
present and the Personage was twenty thousand
miles away. Did Mr. Campbell know the precise
relation to “Grant, father,” of the other Grant ?
We do not think he did ; but the Personage did ;
of that we are convinced.
How, an impatient reader may here ask, could
Mr. Campbell who, as said above, personally mus-
tered the convicts, upon landing, remain ignorant
of the relationship if the Grants were respectively
specified as father and son ? Wait.
* * *
There were two parties made up for the
hospital daily—one at 8 a.m., and one on night
muster. If a man took seriously ill between 5.80
p.m. and 8 a.m., it was a highly irregular pro-
ceeding on his part, and one distinctly to be dis-
countenanced. Therefore, the System would not
provide for his removal between these hours. Old
Grant, however, being wise, had made his illness
manifest in time to be sent with the “ night lot.”
And accordingly, a freed constable dragged him,
not unkindly, to the Rum Hospital next door.
There we shall leave him. He concerns us no
more—directly.
# # #
At ten o’clock, the night-constable on
duty at the entrance-gate—he had two watchmen
selected from the prisoners to help him—passed
up into No. 5 dormitory. The door leading into
the courtyard was not locked, nor were the doors
of the several rooms. This was one of the eccen-
tricities of the period for which there is no
accounting. As a rule, there was a superfluity of
locks and bolts, but Hyde Park Barracks, from
1819 to 1826 were never locked at night. In ’26,
a plot to make a sortie on the sleeping town was
discovered, and then the System invested
<£l3 10s. 9d. in padlocks of new and impregnable
design. Which were all picked within a week.
But this is by the way.
The night-constable, Thomas Crake, transport
per the Three Bees on that fatal trip when the
mortality was so great as to shock even the
indurated sensibilities of a British statesman,
passed from the court-yard into No. 5. Up two
flights of stairs went he, ghoul-like in the crawling
pat-pat of his list-slippered feet. And at the
door of No. 5 he stopped and touched the dozing
watchman of the room on the shoulder. “ Grant’s-
son—wake up, Grant’s-son. The old man’s a-
slippin’ ’is wind ! ’E’s got to go ter Horspital,
quick!”
The watchman rubbed his eyes and saluted. It
was only a constable who stood by, but his
fine reverence for authority, coupled with a pro-
found distaste for lashes, suggested the salute.
And so he made it before he moved between the
hammocks to rouse “ Grant, son.”
From floor to roof ran stanchions of timber —a
row against the walls, another row seven feet dis-
tant towards the middle of the room. Across the
stanchions were nailed rails or battens, and from
these were suspended the hammocks. Twenty
inches of width space was allowed to each ham-
mock. For some that narrow bed held a fiercer
hell than even the System maintained outside, for
their consciences stirred malevolently even in their
dreams. For others, again, the hammock was
Paradise. These latter drifted back in their
visions to the scenes of innocence; to the spots
untainted by travail of spirit or by sense of injus-
tice or wrong-doing. For a brief space they forgot.
There were others still —some who were awake ;
who never seemed to sleep ; who were perpetually
crying in these night hours after their lost youth
*The last woman hanged in Sydney before the cessation
of transportation was attired in' a green dress.

—the youth perhaps they had never known.
Surely there is a heaven to give some people the
youth they never knew here !
Among these last was young Grant. Amid the
animals who snored off their fatigue, amid the
more delicate souls which sighed remorse or
laughed in their sleep, young Grant was awake.
He could not sleep—prescient, perhaps, of impend-
ing trouble, slumber refused to touch his eyelids.
Before the watchman had reached his hammock
he had started up. In this ward —No. 5 was
good conduct dormitory—it was unusual for the
watchman to patrol after silence-bell; and as he
heard the muffled steps between the sleepers, he
knew instinctively he was wanted. In the same
instant that the watchman muttered his name, he
spoke.
“Grant, son?” The full name slipped off the
tongue, mechanically alert by frequent repetition.
“Me ; yes, I’m awake, Butchy. Is it the—old
man ?”
“Yes, sonny—Grant, father, be bad. So Con-
st’ble Crake, he ses.”
The young fellow leapt out of the hammock
instantly.
“Yer’ll get into it, a-sleepin’in yer ducks an’
shirt, some time, my lad, if you don’t care.”
But the words passed unheeded. “Grant, son,”
was at the door.
“Oh, Mr Crake .”
“W’y don’t yer s’lute?” growled the officer,
tetchy even in that hour, and though he stood on
the verge of an infamy, as to a fancied slight.
The lad saluted. And then, quivering with an
alarm he did not try to disguise, he pressed hia
enquiry.
“Mr. Crake, is father —is he worse?”
’long, and I’ll tell yer !”
* * *
Down the stairs they passed, into the
courtyard. As they paused at the last step Con-
stable Crake clutched the waist of young Grant
and said :
“I know all ’bout yer, m’ lady I I’ve bin
waitin’ for this chance for a long time!”
“Oh God !—but Mr. Crake, my father —is he
worse, is he dyin’
“ your father! Think I care anythin’
’b6ut the ol’ lag ?”
Young Grant tried to wrest the clasped arm
free.
“No yer don’t! Yer’ve to lis’en to wot I’ve
got ter say.”
“ Is —he—worse ?”
“ I dunno, an’ don’t care ! ’E can die as quick
as lightning for all I care. It’s on’y yer as I care
for, m’ beauty !” He flung his free arm round the
neck of the other and kissed young Grant’s
cheeks.
“Leave go ! leave go ! or I’ll cry out!”
“No yer won’t, m’ beauty! If there’s any
public row ’bout this the ’Thorities ’ll ’ave ter
take it up, an’ then, m’ beauty, yer’ll all be in for
it—th’ brother yer tried ter save by cornin’ out in
his place, an’ yer father fer lyin’ ’bout yer, an’
yerself fer a-deceivin’ of Guv’munt!”
“ O, God !”
“’E can’t ’elp yer! There ain’t no God out
’ere—we all left God be’ind us in th’ Channel,”
“What d’ye —want? ’ Young Grant was
gasping.
“Ah, oh, so an’ pretty’s a-comin’ to reason, is
she? That’s right! I’ll be a friend to’er then.
An’ that’s all I want, ter be a friend to yer ? Yer
understand, don’t yer, m’ beauty?”
The lamp by the gateway was flashing a quaver-
ing beam into the courtyard. By it Mr. Crake
saw her lips quiver, but he could not hear words.
They were an assent though—at least, he reasoned
so.
“That’s right, m’ pretty. Now, yer just got
ter come to me three times a week, while yer ol’
man’s in horspital. An’ ’e ain’t a-goin’ter come
out in an ’urry either. I heard the report ter-night—
he may live five years, hut’ll never do work agen—
’e’s goin’ looney ”
“Oh ! ” The cry of smitten love rang
through the stillness. But there was no echo, the
distantly receding footsteps of the patrol marched
down by the watch-house, and answered the
challenge of the sentry there by “ All’s well! ”
Crake stopped her mouth with his hand.
“Shout agen, yer ’ussy, and I’ll blow th’ gaff
termorrer on yer all—an’ th’ Gov’nor can’t but
’elp then but get that b’utiful brother of yourn
rested arter all. An’ yer dad won’t be too looney
to flog—nobody’s too looney to flog. An’ as for
yer, wot yer refuse me, wot I ax perlite like, yer’ll
’ave ter give ter everybody that axes—beaks an’
sodgers, an’ constables. They ain’t goin’ ter let a
purty piece o’ goods like yer slip through their
’ands. An’ ’tis better for yer ter ’av one friend ’n
not fifty. An’ I don’t l’ll marry yer, but I
don’t mind a-tryin’ ter get yer ’signed ter me. An’
then yer’ll be in clover, m’ missy !” He stopped
to take breath, and to emit an anticipatory chuckle
at the sensation which would be caused at the
transformation of “Grant, son,” into “Tom
Crake’s ’signed ’ooman !” But some laughter is
premature.
* * #
Before his cackle ended, the girl had
recovered something of that presence of mind and
that readiness of resource which had enabled her,
in the first instance, to deceive the hulk-keeper at
Plymouth as she substituted herself for the twin-
brother, who was wanted at home to keep the
mother and the younger children from starving—
and then, for the succeeding fifteen months, to
have evaded discovery by ship-mates, by con-
stables, and by fellow-convicts. And with that
restoration to self-command, she revealed a sudden
coyness.
“Yes, Mr. Crake! I see what you mean!
An’, o’ course, it ’ud be better ter have only—one
friend—than to be—but you’ll keep th’ secret,
Mr. Crake ?”
“O’ course, m’ pretty ! I don’t allow no other
chaps to sit down in my nest!”
“Then, Mr. Crake, you can—do as you like-
to-morrow night!”
“No, you don’t!” He swore a great oath.
“To-night or—l’ll peach termorrer.”
“I’ll swear to you, Mr. Crake—l’ll swear to
you I’ll give myself to you to-morrow night. In
the mess-room—my week for night messing begins
to-morrow, an’ I take the key an’ give it to Mr.
Grove. An’ I’ll not lock the —door, y’ see—only
pretend to.”
And Crake, whose conduct this night was the
fruit of an impulse suddenly originated by the
news communicated in the gossip of the guard-
room of old Grant’s illness, was reluctantly com-
pelled to admit there was a wisdom in “Young
Grant’s ” suggestion. But he swore, with fearful
oaths, that he would not only disclose her secret
but would drag her to the gutters if she failed in
her appointment.
She tendered him a kiss in proof of her sin-
cerity, and was mounting the stairs again when a
thought struck her, and quickly she ran back.
“ Mr. Crake, Mr. Crake !” she half-whispered,
half-cried. He, nearing the lodge, heard her and
returned.
“ Yes? —so yer thought better o’ it ?”
She took no heed, but went on with her ques-
tion.
“Mr. Crake, I’ll—give you another kiss now —
two, or as many as you want —if—if—ye’ll only
tell me how —you found me out?”
“Found yer out, m’ dear? W’y. I suspected
—l’m an ol’ searcher at the Factory,* m’beauty,
an’ yer ain’t th’ first ’ooman by ’arf-a-dozen who’s
come out for ’er man or ’er father, or ’er brother
—an’ w’en I suspected yer, wot d’ye think I did ?”
The girl was silent; but he laughed long,
though subduedly,
“ W’y, I gave yer ol’ man a bit o’ bacoy now an’
then- an’ ’e peached on yer at last! ” Again he
laughed.
“My father—l’ll never believe it! ”
“ Oh, but he did, m’ beauty ! An’ now for them
kisses. , . .
* * *
"When, the next morning but one, the
cooks entered the mess-room, they solved a trifling
difficulty which had harassed the convict watch-
men at the gate. What had become of Constable
Crake? He had left the lodge about midnight
and had not returned.
The cooks discovered why he had not returned.
He lay on one of the mess-tables with his throat
cut.
* In 1815, male constables were employed as searchers
at the Female Factory, Parramatta.
During one whole week last term no
senior counsel held a brief in N.S.W. Supreme
Court. This is attributed to an attempt at fru-
gality on the part of litigants,
A misguided man named Darcy wandered
into the kitchen at St. George’s Presbytery,
Carlton, Melb., the other day, and after taking
off all his clothes he squatted in a corner. He
subsequently explained that his clothes were red-
hot and he had taken them off to cool. He also
believed that the devil was after him, but thought
he would be safe near the church. He was
remanded for medical ti’eatment. There can be
no question about the mental condition of the man
who thinks to avoid the devil by going to church.
Colonel “Fire-low” Price is an aggressive
aspirant for the office of Victorian Chief Com-
missioner of Police. When the Victorian
Government want to commit suicide, they had
better appoint to the Chief Commissionership this
bumptious swashbuckler, whose action during the
maritime strike has damned him for all time with
the common people. Price started in life with the
heavy handicap, from a democratic point of view,
of his father’s official reputation, and during
strike-time he voluntarily added to that burden.
The attitude of Chief Commissioner Chondey (by
the way, the handsomest, as he is one of the most
humane men in the Victorian public service) was
exactly the reverse, and the good relations existing
between the workmen and the police formed one of
the few pleasing incidents of a pitiful period.

There’s a Bunk in the Humpy.
THE SPLITTER’S SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bush was too lonely—the life was too slow
And, Johnny, my son, to the city would go ;
He knew that his father was lonely and grey,
And he might have gone there without running
away.
There’s a bunk in the humpy—a glass on the shelf
Which have never been used since he used them
himself,
And that bunk in the humpy will stand till he
comes
To his father’s old hut in the depth of the gums.
’Tis true that my temper was soured long ago,
But old men have sorrows that sons do not know ;
I “jawed” him one day when my temper was
stirred,
An’ he left his old father with never a word.
There’s a bunk in the humpy, &c.
Did he think it was kind—did he think it was
right
To the lonely old man in the humpy that night?
Who sat with the sound of the rain in his ears,
And thought till his eyes ran a banker with tears?
There’s a bunk in the humpy, &c.
His mattress and pillow and bluey are there—
He’ll never sleep sounder on feathers, I’ll swear,
Or eat better stews than I warmed by the blaze
’Neath the old chimney gutter on cold, rainy days
There’s a bunk in the humpy, &c.
An’ should he come back when the old man is out
He never need linger a moment in doubt :
He’ll know where the key of the padlock is hid,
An’ there’s grub in the gin-case for lifting the lid.
There’s a bunk in the humpy, &c.
Sept., ’92.
HENRY LAWSON.

“Dipso.” writes ;
Dear Bulletin,— You have been “had” over the par.
pretending to embody a letter received recently by a
N.S.W. gold-mine secretary. It is an old wheeze. Your
contributor has amended it a little, but I had that old
chestnut in Adelaide Lantern as far back as ’81; and
even then it was stolen from an exchange.— The Dipso-
maniac.
Which is a somewhat remarkable letter to receive,
in view of the following statement just made in
Bathurst (N.S.W.) National Advocate
The Bulletin boasts that it never scissors without
acknowledgment. Well, the following is not acknow-
ledged, and it is merely a copy, bar the line commencing
“ bismuth ” and ending “ trace,” of a par. written by the
present writer three years ago in a Sydney paper that was
certainly not The Bulletin.
Then follows the same old par. Have we come to
this? Have we lived to be accused of plagiarising
Nelson P. Whitelocke ?

have Tim Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for

city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
■in the hands of our carriers, with whom ice do not wish
to interfere.

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-

Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 12, 1892.
The Impotence of Parliaments.
In the opinion of this House the powers of Cabinet
Governments have increased, are increasing, and ought
to be diminished.— Motion by Rose, M.L A., in New
South Wales Assembly.
The above motion, which is stranded some-
where—it matters very little where—on the
notice-paper of the New South Wales Assembly,
is a somewhat badly-expressed effort to draw
attention to one of the greatest difficulties of
the present so-called system of representative
government. Mr. Rose’s proposition may
never be heard of again to any useful pur-
pose, because under the existing scheme of
administration a private member’s motion may
remain on the business paper of the House till
the world grows old without the slightest
chance of its coming on for discussion, or with-
out the discussion —should there be one—lead-
ing to any*results ; but all the same it expresses
the great evil of Australian politics. The
system of parliamentary government—though
hardly anybody seems to be aware of the fact—
is as dead as anything well can be. Parliament
does not govern any longer ; it never did
govern to any great extent, but now any fleet-
ing vestige of authority which it once possessed
is utterly gone. The nation—alleged to be the
last court of appeal and the one whose decision
is absolutely final—does not govern either. The
Cabinet has all the authority which exists, and
that, despite Mr. Rose’s dictum, is not so much
as is generally supposed. Tt is an oligarchy
tempered by the dead weight of chaos; a species
of seven-headed autocrat, whose tyranny is
limited by the fact that he has a leaden load
permanently attached to him, so that he can
get round fast enough to be obnoxious, though
not fast enough to be absolutely deadly. The
Ministry, in fact, is blocked by the loquacity of
the Legislature, and but for that it would be
practically absolute. The other elements of the
“ Constitution ” are mere ciphers.
* # *
In the first place, the nation has practically
no voice in the selection of a Government. It
is graciously allowed to choose between Pakkes
and Dibbs; or between Moreuead, the squatter
and cheap-labour man, and Tomuel Griffil-
wkaith, the two-headed slave-dealer ; or
between Shiels, the humble flunkey of the
rascally bankers of Melbourne, and the fat-
headed Duncan Gillies. It can’t gel rid of
them, for when once the “representative”
political incubus becomes reasonably ancient
and deep-rooted, then even if ninety-nine con-
stituencies reject him, there is always a hun-
dredth one somewhere which sends him back to
the Legislature. There is abundant provision
for putting the old party hacks into the House,
and no provision worth mentioning for keeping
them out. The country can’t dictate their
policy, for it has to answer in one word —
yes or no—on forty or fifty questions at
once, and the situation is just the same as that
of a jury which has to decide simultaneously on
a case of murder, one of bigamy, another of
trespass, a problem concerning a bottomry
bond, a brutal assault, and an action for breach-
of-promise of marriage and adultei’ation of
sugar, combined with a land title problem, two
divorces, and the settlement of the list of con-
tributories in six broken banks and a building
society of which the books have been lost.
And if this same jury had simply to give one
answer, in the negative or the affirmative,
covering all these problems at once; and it
couldn’t give damages in the breach-of-promise
suit without hanging the alleged homicide, and
granting both divorces, and making all the
bank shareholders liable for calls, and finding
for the plaintiff in the land dispute, and
deciding that the sugar was adulterated, it
would exactly express the situation. There-
fore, the country, being unable to give expres-
sion to its opinions in any coherent shape,
simply hands over the whole business of
governing to a certain number of politicians,
and abdicates its own authority utterly.
* ** *
Then the Legislature, under exactly the same
kind of compulsion, proceeds to abdicate its
authority in similar fashion. In the average
Parliament there are almost always two lead-
ing fossils who maintain their positions because
tbere is absolutely no certain means of getting
rid of them until they are dead. Each repre-
sents a party, and each party is afraid to depose
its own particular fossil, lest he and his two
or three personal followers should immediately
join the other side as a matter of vengeance.
Politics has no fury like a deposed fossil. Also
the great axiom of party politics is that it is
unsafe to swap fossils while crossing a stream,
and the party, under present conditions, is
always crossing a stream and never getting
over. The Governor—an irresponsible indivi-
dual over whom the nation has no control—
“sends for” the one of these two public
burdens which has the biggest following, and
he thereupon proceeds to form a Ministry at his
own discretion without consulting the House or
the country. If the House doesn’t like any one
Minister out of the team it has the option of
either putting up with him, or discharging the
whole crowd and going back on its political
principles at the same time —as for the country
it has practically no option at all until the next
general election, and even then it has none
worth mentioning. If it adopts the latter
course the Governor thereupon sends for the
other incubus, and he forms a Cabinet in exactly
the same fashion, and if the House doesn’t like
that Cabinet either it has the blessed privilege
of going back to the previous lot—the nation at
large not having any privilege in the case
whatever. When the House has decided which
of the two evils it will endure, the Ministry
presents its “policy ” and the Legislature must
either accept it whole or discharge the Ministry ;
if it kicks against even one item the “ constitu-
tional ” result is that it must go back to the
other gang of placemen, and swallow their
policy whole, under pain of another general
stoppage of the legislative machine.
* # #
In a House of perhaps 120 members the
seven (or thereabouts) who, without the per-
mission of the nation or anybody else, have been
constituted as the Ministry, draw up all
measures of importance, and the other 113
accept them under protest. They are not
allowed even to make any important improve-
ments ; that can only be effected, in most cases,
by defeating the Cabinet in a division, where-
upon that body resigns, and both the original
measure and the improvements are lost in the
confusion, and another seven or eight bosses
come into power and make the Assembly accept
their proposals whole in the same autocratic
fashion. The so-called Legislature isn’t allowed
to legislate at all. It exists only to patch
Ministerial measures, which it isn’t allowed
even to patch effectually; and to butcher
Governments, which it isn’t allowed to do effec-
tually either, for when it has killed the last
Government, then the one that was killed
before rises from its grave and goes on its
nefarious way as of old, until it is solemnly
murdered over again, whereupon the other one
resurrects in its turn ; and so on practically ad
infinitum. In a House of 113 private members and
seven Ministers, the 113 are generally allowed
one night per week on an average for the trans-
action of “private business,” and the seven
occupy all the rest of the time. Under these
circumstances no private measure of any im-
portance is ever passed unless the Cabinet
happens to approve of-it. If the Cabinet does
not approve of it then it is judiciously shuffled
down to the bottom of the business paper, or
otherwise blocked, and so effective is this pro-
cess that the same proposition may loom up
kke a ghost every session for a quarter of a
century, and be no nearer accomplishment at
the end of that period than it was at the
beginning. The only chance for an important
private measure is that sooner or later some
Cabinet which is short of a war-cry will take it
up, and present it as the Cabinet’s own original
idea, and take all the credit of it, and shunt
the originator out into the cold.
# # *
It is generally understood to be one of the
props of the Constitution that the popular
Chamber has sole control of the public funds,
but this, like all the rest, is a delusion. The
Cabinet has control of the public funds, and the
Legislature merely enjoys the privilege of
cursing impotently after the event. In many
cases the Financial Statement is not presented
until the year is far advanced—sometimes not
until it is nearly ended—and then there is not
even a pretence of parliamentary supervision ;
the Cabinet simply reports that the money has
been spent, and the Legislature can no more
argue against that accomplished fact than it
can undo its own past existence, and prevent
itself having been born. If it objects to the
expenditure it can dismiss the Cabinet and get
in its place another Cabinet which will act in
exactly the same fashion, but that is not con-
trolling the public funds in any shape or form.
Sometimes, on the other hand, the Financial
Statement is presented in advance, and then
the House has just about as much control as it
has the other way. It may make a few small
alterations, and pare off a few coppers here
and there, without causing any disaster ;
but if it attempts any large reform,
either in the method of raising or spend-
ing the revenue, it thereby expresses “ want
of confidence ” in the Cabinet, and that
body resigns. Then another Cabinet comes
into power, and if the House endeavours to
supervise the public funds in its case it resigns
also, and so the process may go on until twenty
Cabinets have resigned, and the whole public
service has been at a dead-lock for months,- and
yet the Parliament has no more authority over
the finances than when it started. And even
when the Government nominally submits to
the decision of the House, and withdraws
objectionable items of expenditure, it generally
happens that it simply borrows some trust
funds without authority, and proceeds with the
expenditure all the same ; and when the matter
is reported to the people’s representatives, they
can only curse after the event, as usual.
# * *
The whole system of government is a wild
burlesque, but the method by which the Legisla-
ture attempts to assert its power in financial
matters is probably the most humorous feature
of all. The recent trouble which came upon
the Dibbs (N.S.W.) Ministry is a case in point.
In that instance, the Cabinet was accused of
having taken £lOOO out of the public funds for
the Premier’s private purposes. In other words,
it was charged with malversation of trust-
funds in a bald and glaring form. It was
put upon its trial, and found “ not guilty ”
—principally by its own votes. It went into
the jury-box to consider its own case, and there
returned a unanimous verdict of acquittal in
favour of itself. If Francis Abigail and his
fellow-prisoners in the case of the Australian
Banking Co. had demanded the privilege of
acting as jurors at their own trial, it is probable
that somebody would have observed the
grotesqueness of the situation, but when a
Government does the same no one seems to
dream of objecting. On the general want-of-
confidence motion the Dibbs Cabinet was saved
by a majority of four, which is just about half
its own voting power; in other words, the
Assembly hasn’t confidence in it, and it retains
office merely because half of it has confidence
in itself. Except as a commentary on the
beauties of Party Government, however,
this result matters very little. If the
Dibbs Ministry had.been dismissed because it
jobbed away £lOOO without authority, the
country would only have got in its stead the
old Foreign-trade gang which, inside of six
years jobbed aw r ay over £1500,000 also without
authority, and at the head of it, possibly, the
whining old political impostor who, only the
other day cabled off’, purely on his own authority,
to a friend of his in England an order for
£9OOO worth of useless statues. Moreover, the
£lOOO is gone, and the £600,000 is gone like-
wise, and the Assembly may protest till the
end of Time, but neither amount will ever come
back. The Cabinet, under the present nefarious
system, is master of the situation, except when
it has to sit hopelessly by and be talked to
death by some member of the Neild descrip-
tion ; and the rest of the Legislature and the
nation at large are merely the voice of one
crying in the wilderness, and trying to gather
up the water that was spilt upon the sands the
year before last.
Exit Abigail.
Last w r eek one chapter in the sordid history of
the Australian Banking Co. came to a definite
end, when Francis Abigail, N.S.W. ex-
Minister of Mines, formerly Chairman of
Directors in the defunct institution, was sen-
tenced to five years’ imprisonment, while his
colleague, M‘Nam aka, once manager of the
same so-called bank, was sentenced to seven
years. The Australian Banking Co. was a poor,
mean swindle from first to last -a concern in
which the accounts were clumsily botched by
cobblers and tenth-rate speculators who had
neither sufficient principle to be honest nor
sufficient brains to be brilliant rogues j
and Abigail was, in many respects, the type
and lilting emblem of the institution. The
bank, so far as can lie gathered, had not
one single redeeming feature in its character—
it was a joint-stock lie and a cheap deception
all through. Its chairman was probably just
about as honest and just about as dishonest as,
at least, half of ordinary mankind—which is by
no means an overstrained compliment ; and it
was chiefly his colossal vanity, the outcome of
his unaccountable success, which brought him to
ruin. Despite his ill-deeds, however, many will
extend to him some small measure of sympathy,
if only on account of the stupendous nature of
his fall. A little while ago he was a capitalist
of some note, a member of the Legisla-
cure, a financier -of a sort an influentia-
citizen, a bright and shining light of the church
a pillar and prop of one of the great sectarian
divisions, a Minister of State, a buttress of tha
Foreign-trade party —and now, what remains?
# # #
Both among financiers and politicians Abigail
was an anomaly. He had a reputation for cast-
iron honesty, chiefly because of his merciless
attacks upon evil-doers, and partly because it was
surely hard to imagine what such a man could
have to live for unless he was honest. His ignor-
ance and his vanity were both enormous, and
that such a person should have been a success
in public life is a solemn commentary on
the social and political institutions of Aus-
tralia. The method of Abigail’s rise was
characteristic of him, and of his party.
He was an industrious tradesman, a peddling
religious fanatic, a little suburban alderman
with a great volume of ungrammatical elo-
quence, then a member of Parliament—member
for the besieged city of Londonderry of two
centuries ago —a politician whose entire creed
was summed up in the theory that the Pope
was going up and down Macquarie-street,
Sydney, like a roaring lion seeking whom he
might devour, and an ardent Foreign-trader
because, having met a lot of Irish Catholics
who were Protectionists, it at once became a
fixed idea in his narrow intelligence that Pro-
tection (to which he had once given his entire
adherence) was the short road to another
massacre of St. Bartholomew'. Then, in an
evil hour, he was induced to add banking to his
other sins, and banking, run on shoemaking
principles, was his ruin.
# * #
As a Minister he was a dire and hopeless
failure, but as a private member he did enormous
service. He looked even more honest than an
honest man generally does look. He was eager,
hungry, watchful the human epitome of a
rat peering out of a sewer ;he w 7 as a sleepless
being always bristling for a fight. Nobody
had a keener scent for a fraud, or exposed
it more relentlessly. His forte lay in digging
the heart out of a swindle, and holding it up,
impaled on his gaunt and haggard finger,
for public execration. Then the little,
wizened man, whose gnarled intelligence had
grown sideways, and who dug into the
mysteries of tilings, always expecting, with
the eye of faith, to find the Jesuits at the
bottom, used to grow almost eloquent, and his
incredible tenacity made him a power in the
impotent and riotous Assembly which howled
and bayed round the Jenmngs-Dibbs Adminis-
tration. In these times he even acquired a
certain reputation for sagacity, and either be-
cause Pakkes supposed him to be intelligent,
or, far more likely, because Pakkes knew that
he —this uninstructed shoemaker—was tractable
and not intelligent, that ancient schemer re-
warded him with a portfolio. Then Abigail’s
political bubble burst. In his ministerial situ-
ation he was a mere irascible automaton ;
and if the “ minits ” which disfigured his
career in the Cabinet had been put in
at his trial instead of the so-called “evi-
dence as to character,” to say nothing of the
evidence given as to his intelligence, it would
have been much better for him. His business
reputation was based entirely on his infinite
capacity for going round on all-fours looking
for a dropped half-penny. He seems to have
taken his own measure at last when at the trial
he pleaded his copious ignorance as practically
his oidy defence, and it would be interesting to
know what gross injury or insult done by him
upon Pakkes caused that old gentleman to
blast his former colleague in the witness box bv
swearing that he was a man of strong intelli
gence, thus destroying the only plea which held
out any hope of Abigail’s salvation,
* * #
But though few will seriously maintain that
Francis Abigail’s conviction was unjust, or
that his sentence was mort severe than the case
required, in view of the ruin brought by him
upon so many homes, it is the general, though
as yet publicly unexpressed, opinion that tin
manner in which justice was meted out to hiir
is open to much question. It w'as Abigail’s
misfortune to be tried before Mr. Justice Win-
deyer, audit is Justice Windbyer’s misfortune
to have the instincts of the hunter rather than
of the unimpassioned Judge much too strongly
developed in his composition. The average
jury can be driven before a strong-minded and
impetuous Judge like twelve sheep before a
collie, and the whirlwind of his Honor’s
passion is a potent factor in obtaining con-
victions. Abigail’s conviction was probably
just in every respect, and it must be con-
fessed that the Judge’s summing-up, how-
ever slaughterous, was remorselessly logical ;
but if Abigail had been an innocent man the
manner of the charge to the jury might have
brought about a conviction all the same. A
judicial deliverance ■to a jury ought to be
a calm, cold, and deliberate summary of every
point both for and against the prisoner, de-
livered by a calm and passionless man who
shows no definite desire either for acquittal or
for conviction, but only for judicial deliverance.
In the present case, it was a cyclone of burning
indignation a speech for the prosecution
delivered by a skilled and bitter advocate—
such a speech as J eeereys might have delivered
in those better days when he was only a
brilliant and hysterical lawyer who could shed
genuine tears at a tale of genuine woe. If
the mild and gentle language of Rogers,
Q.C., on behalf of the prosecution had come
from the bench, and that of Judge
Windeyer had been discharged at the
bar, the situation would have been much
more becoming. Judge Windeyer is unques-
tionably the soundest lawyer on the New South
Wales Bench, and in intricate civil cases,
■where there is no gaol or gallows in the dis-
tance, he has no superior in the Australasian
judiciary ; but his infirmity of temper, and his
unfortunate instincts as a born prosecutor rather
than as a Judge designed to uphold the digni-
fied equanimity of the ideal Bench, make
him eminently unfitted to preside in a criminal
court. Abigail, despite his monstrous ignorance,
his diseased vanity, t he fanaticism which makes
him overlook things near and pertinent, while
he glares after imaginary Popish conspiracies
afar off, and any other features in his character


which might partly account for his conduct,
was guilty ia a legal sense; but the style of
Judge Windeyer’s tempestuous oratory might
easily have induced many a weak-kneed jury to
convict him even had his case been less
obviously hopeless.
* * #
The outer world has now practically seen the
last of Francis Abigail. To a poor cripple of
his miserably weak physique, five years’ hard
labour is so nearly equivalent to a sentence of
death that the difference is hardly appreciable.
But even if he should outlive his term, lie will
never ascend to notoriety again. Both his rise
and his fall are due, in a great measure, to his
old chief, who has been for nearly a generation
the leading spirit in New South Wales politics,
and has, by his neglect of domestic legislation,
permitted that province to be or become the
paradise of quack bankers, quack doctors, bogus
lotteries, and all other sorts and varieties of
shams. It was Parkes’ Kiama Ghost policy
which made it possible for a man to attain
high office simply because he was the boss
crank in an Orange institution. It was Parkes’
neglect of his most obvious duties which
allowed the existence of such establish-
ments as the Australian Banking Co. It was
Parkes who made Abigail a “ statesman,” and
thus made his name of special value to “ cronk”
financial institutions. And it was also Parkes
who drove the last nail in his disused col-
league’s coffin by deposing to his intelligence
at a moment when a charge of intelligence was
about as fatal to him as a charge of dynamite.
The very fact of his calling the ex-Premier as a
witness should have acquitted the fallen finan-
cier of any suspicion of having enough sagacity
to come in out of the rain.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A “Long-felt Want.”
One of the noticeable features of the banking
troubles in Australia is that almost every
financier who has gone to gaol—-and they are
many has relied largely upon evidence
as to character. Just when the wreck-
age of his bursted bank, and the fraudu-
lent balance-sheet have satisfactorily demon-
strated that the only character the defendant
ever had is one which ought to be removed to a
noxious trades site, six or eight clergymen and
other “ respectable ” members of the com-
munity, who once saw the back of his head in
an omnibus, have wandered into the witness-
box to testify, as an extenuating circumstance,
that he is a person of the highest morals
ami has always worn the white flower of a
blameless life. The fact that everybody with
any money—even if it is obviously some one
else’s money —always gets this kind of support,
and that hardly anybody without money ever
gets it, leads The Bulletin to imagine that it
is practically a saleable article, and therefore it
seems desirable that a company should be
formed to supply it regularly at a certain fixed
tariff. This institution would fill quite a lot of
long-felt wants. It would supply witnesses to
testify to the character of anybody who would
pay to have his character testified to, and
would keep assorted evidence on hand—clergy-
men, doctors, C.M.C.s, business men, and
others. The fact that these individuals were
connected with the company would be kept
strictly secret and they would be scattered all
over the country ; thus if a man were tried,
say at Townsville, he could have a white-haired
clergyman who had come express from Wil-
cannia to state, in a voice broken by emotion,
that he had known the suspect from his
innocent boyhood, and that his reputation
was beyond reproach. The emotion and
travelling expenses would be extras. Also
the company would supply the aged mother
who would weep silently in the court,
and faint just before the jury went out to
consider its verdict, and thus touch a responsive
chord which would be charged for in the bill —
“ Responsive chord, 10s. fid.” It would furnish
testimonials from men of good standing to per-
sons in search of situations, and would act as a
reference in life insurance cases and swear that
the insurer was sober, healthy, and came of a
long-lived family. Any citizen whose character
showed signs of fading or who wanted an adver-
tisement, and thought that a banquet and an
address tendered by a number of leading citizens,
gathered together to express their spontaneous
admiration of his worth, would help him in
any way, could leave his order and his
cheque at the company’s office, and the banquet
and the leading citizens would be supplied.
Presentations of plate would be arranged for ;
applause, laughter and encores furnished at
meetings and theatres; letters written to
patent-medicine vendors from patients whose
livers, before taking the nostrum, had been
swollen up to a fearful size ; public men abused
in the papers, so that they could come forward
afterwards and demonstrate the utter baseness
of the calumny, and thereby get a good puff;
crowds always kept on hand to rush into banks
and lodge large sums of money, in order to
excite confidence, also enthusiastic depositors
to rise up when the institution suspended and
say that they were ready to wait ten years for
the money, and friendly creditors to uprear
themselves at bankruptcy meetings and
announce unanimously that, owing to the
high character and undeserved misfortunes
of the insolvent, they would take 3s. fid. in the £
payable by unsecured bill at 36 months.
Browning citizens would likewise be laid on,
who would be rescued at the imminent risk of
his life by anybody who wanted to make a
favourable impression on the community;
speeches and sermons written, interruptions
fixed up, and impromptus furnished to crush
the interrupter, bouquets hove, banks burgled
so as to have the books out of the way before
the audit, converts kept on hand to weep at
revival meetings and heave cheques and bank-
notes on the platform in aid of the cause, and
medical certificates and alibis put in. If such a
company as this wouldn’t supply a great want,
then The Bulletin has altogether misunder-
stood the meaning of that oft-quoted expression.
Bear Bulletin, -yin a recent article you com-
mented on the frightful waste of Labour funds
caused by strikes, and you have often pointed
out that there is a brilliant opening in each of the
Australian capitals for a thorough-going, honest
democratic daily. But would such a paper,
privately owned, be a success? Recent London
experience says no. Several labour, democratic
and socialistic organs have been “ frozen out,”
or bought out by Capital there during the last
two or three years. Capitalists can always
bide their time to snap up any successful
democratic paper and turn it Tory. Where could
the labour-unions better employ their funds
than in running democratic dailies in Sydney,
Melbourne, Adelaide and Brisbane ? Even if
there was an annual loss for the first few years,
it would be money well lost. Nowadays, no
cause can progress fast unless it has press
organs, which are at once its sword and shield.
To ensure safety and continuous existence, the
ownership of Labour dailies would have to be
vested in the Trades Councils of the various
capitals. The main reason why so ,many
workers —clerks, shopmen, small farmers, etc.—
are inclined to listen to the bray of the
“ National Ass,” is simply ignorance as to
their own interests, and the position they hold
towards landlords, sweaters, and the money-
spinning fraternity in general, and nothing
would dispel this ignorance so fast as a demo-
cratic daily. The Broken Hill strike is said to
have cost the miners £300,000 in wages alone,
without counting the loss to the labour funds
by subscriptions from all parts of Australia. A
third of the money lost would have started
great dailies—heralds of the coming dawn —in
each Australian capital.—Yours, X.Y.Z.
Apropos the possession by the Crown of a jury-
list not given to the defence in the Broken Hill
strike leaders’ trial, it is stated that policemen
were sent round the Deniliquin district to
ascertain the opinions of the jurors summoned.
Mr. “ Jack ” Wagner, (who appeared with Mr.
Want for the defence) through residence at his
father’s station, PerricoOta, knew some of the
jurors, but the bias of thought of the majority
was.an unknown quantity to the defence, and
a known quantity to the Crown. At this
juncture the Rev. Mr, Allen, who was in Court
and saw the unfairness of the whole proceed-
ings, stepped in and offered his advice. In
consequence, the defence challenged 21 jurors.
The result, however, proves that the Village
Priest was not strong in striking a panel. The
squattocracy and its tail at Deniliquin talk of
driving Mr. Allen out of the place for his
action, but we should hope there are enough
good Presbyterians appreciative of noble motives
who will give him a good call elsewhere.
The usual cablegrams from England announce
that the blessed Times approves of the sen-
tences passed on the Broken Hill strike leaders,
and also of Abigail’s conviction. This sort of
thing is getting very stale, and it seems a pity
that the cablegram-man doesn’t give it a rest.
Nobody in Australia cares one long beer
whether the Times approves or not, and even
in England people have lost their interest in the
Times’ approval. Besides, that heavy two-
penn’orth is so absolutely mechanical in its
action that anybody can tell what its senti-
ments will be without the expense of a
cablegram. It always approves of arrests,
convictions, and hangings, and of any-
thing bearing the outward semblance of
‘ ‘ loyalty. ” It cordially endorses Soudan con-
tingents, increased military expenditure, land-
grarrt railways, syndicates, the suppression of
strikes, and things like that, and it backs up
everything that is likely to commend itself to a
“right-thinking person ” generally. Australia
is growing so tired of this that the next strike-
leader who gets into trouble should call
evidence to show that the Times is likely to
approve of his conviction. If that wouldn’t
secure his acquittal, nothing would.
The New South Wales police have at last
began to discover where those mysterious
infants —the ones which are advertised for in
the evening papers —go to. At all events,
seven of them have been dug up in a back-yard
at Macdonaldtown (Sydney), and how many
more remain to be dug up in other back-yards
is a matter for conjecture. Competition has
brought prices so low in the infant industry that
the ordinary artist in babies can’t afford
doctors’ certificates and funerals, and as the
adopted offspring has to die, else the
business wouldn’t pay, and it must be
disposed of somehow, the chances are that the
back-yard system of interment is tolerably com-
mon. At all events, it should be worth while to
investigate. If the detectives would get on the
trail of all the notorious baby-farmers, and pro-
ceed to dig up the back-yard of every one of the
tribe, the results might be surprising. The
Macdonaldtown artists didn’t get their idea
from Deeming as they were obviously in the
business before his time, but probably the
police did, as this is the first time they have
gone systematically to work to disturb the
baby-farmer’s little private cemetery.
+. .
The “ Great Gulf Robbery ” is to be perpetrated
at once, if possible. The stick-at-nothing
Cbiffilwraith Government proposes, if neces-
sary, to hold a special supplementary session of
the dying Parliament, for the sole purpose of
ratifying the land-grant-robbery railway con-
tracts, the drafts of which will then be all
ready. The new gagging Standing Orders will
prevent the two or three remaining honest
politicians in the House from stonewalling, and
Parliament, with the death-rattle in its throat,
will be asked to sign away the people’s inheri-
tance for ever. As regards the Gulf lines, what
cares the South? This is the last chance it
has to handle for its own profit the glorious
mineral lands of the North, and it intends to
hold that chance with bulldog grip. Next
year, when the North takes over its own busi-
ness, it will also have to take over all existing
agreements. Civil wars have grown out of
smaller swindles. >

The S.M. Herald asserts that it cannot be
the duty of the Government to. find work for
the unemployed, because “ Every other class
accepts it as a necessity as well as a duty to
rely on itself. ” This is one of these sophisms
that are apt to deceive those who skim
over the surface of things. The “ unem-
ployed ” is not a fixed quantity, nor is it
a class; it is constantly recruited, not only
from the ranks of the, manual workers, but
from every other section of the community.
The ruined squatter, publican, baker, pawn-
broker, banker, doctor, lawyer (who can’t
get a fresh start), throws himself at
once on an overstocked labour market ,
to depreciate wages by giving his incapable
service at any price that may be . offered. t
Cheapness being, to some people, a desideratum,
he may displace a better man, or, failing to find ,
work, mgty- join that section of the unemployed t
who constantly seek solace for their woes in send-' ;
ing deputations to interview the Government. .
Thus it becomes evident that the statement .
that “ every other class ” except the vvorkers,
. “.relies on itself” refers only to them during
; periods of prosperity. In their adversity they .
make things much worse for habitual wage-
; earners, and battle even more lustily for
Government billets than any other members of
the community. ,

That raid upon Melbourne “ totes” was quite
characteristic of the law. The totes, rascally
swindles for the most part, were winked at .
(and probably sweated) by the men in blue until
circumstances compelled them to stop the game.
Warrants having been taken out the obviously
proper course was to go round immediately and
arrest every man who kept a machine on the
premises. If the object of the law is the pre-
vention of tote-gambling, the duty of the police
was to seize the plant before it could be further
operated with. But “the authorities ” never
miss a chance to victimise the public under
cover of vindicating the law. So the raid was
arranged to come off on Derby night in order
that a number of poor devils might be legally
robbed of the money they had won. This free
and easy seizing of cash by officers who can give
their own account of it is open to grave objec-
tion, anyway. When they deliberately plan
the seizure of as much cash as possible, under
circumstances which seem to make it impossible
to check the amount that was grabbed, the
objection is very strong indeed.
The bill, introduced by “Andy” Kelly, to
extend the protection of the Employers’ Liability
Act to all seamen within New South Wales
jurisdiction, has been emasculated in the
nominee Council by a select committee of
squatters and shipowners whose votes should
be challenged if they dare to vote against the
second reading, on the ground that they are
personally and pecuniarily interested in the
defeat of the bill. The force of this contention
is exemplified by their cunning alteration of the
definition of the term “ship” so as to exclude any
vessel that is not registered hi the province. This
alteration allows all the wool-carriers to escape
scot free : the bill will not reach the very ships
on which the bulk of the accidents occur.
Again, the term “seamen” has been amended
so as to include only those on a ship who have
signed articles. Under the bill, as originally
drawn, if a personal injury were caused to a
seaman when his ship was “within the jurisdic-
tion of New South Wales,” he could claim the
protection of the principal Act. But as it is
amended, unless “ a ship is moored or at anchor
receiving or discharging . cargo or . an,
injured seaman has'no redress whatever. And
even if life is injured while his ship is receiving
or discharging cargo or coals he is denied what-
ever privilege or advantage he enjoyed under
thfe Merchant Shipping Act, It is superfluous
to say that Crick’s" amendment to compel the
owner to prove absence of negligence has been
ruthlessly erased from the bill.. Dr. Garran,
’in whose charge the bill was, is no longer in the
Council.

Sydney Y.M.C.A, evidently resents Abi-
gail’s conviction and laments concerning “men
held in the highest esteem in the city who had
recently, by reason of a first false step, been called
upon’to suffer at the hands of the law.” “A
first false step” is a mild way of expressing a
swindle of at least four years’ duration. Four
years is a pretty long “step.”
Assuming it to be proved that Sleath and
his fellow-“conspirators ” countenanced and even
directed the stopping of free-labourers by the
pickets at Broken Hill, how does that justify the
sentence? Unquestionably it is a breach of the
law to forcibly prevent a man going to work,
but is it the sort of crime to be punished by two
years’ hard labour? Would any man but a strike-
leader get such a sentence for it, even if he
used a pickhandle to effect his object? And
if a man gets that sentence because he is
a strike leader, then he is illegally punished for
being engaged in an occupation quite as lawful as
sitting on the bench of the Supreme Court.
Further, suppose any picket had used a pick-
handle, and after being duly dealt with by the
law for the assault had been presented by
Sleath and his colleagues with the weapon
silver-mounted accompanied by an illuminated
address, would not the Crown-Prosecutor have
cheerfully, in case of need, abandoned all the
other evidence, and relied on that one fact alone
to prove a blatant sympathy with crime, and a
direct encouragement and incitement to in-
timidation and violence? Then what is the
reason that there has been no prosecution
of the persons who silver-mounted the weapon
used by bank-manager Cotton in his assault
on Sleath, and presented it with ostenta-
tious approbation to the* convicted assailant?
The only reason that can be suggested is that
Cotton’s friends didn’t happen to be unionists.
■ Arresting them would not have done any good ;
it would not have helped to bust up the strike. ,

IN MEMORIAM.
■The firey steed when young was full of go
Rut that was very many years ago. '
His jumping powers had gained >for him renown t. 1
When ridden py. a youth from Perry town.
But O ! the'thought now makes me feel heartsore
To think the steed will jump for him no more.
The horse was known as “ Play-boy ” very appropriate
But to explain the’reason I cannot now relate.
Tho’ as an instance of his sterling worth
At the same place he was foaled he passed away from carta
Many a good steed has changed owners by the score
But this one had the same until he was no more.
To trace his pedigree would lead you back
To England’s earliest record of racer’s crack.
Altho this horse was used in many a way
In all he worked as hearty as boys at play.
He fell asdike a War horse in,his gore :
■And sad to think he’ll jump again no more.
Whilst doing duty,on tjie campjng; ground
With cows and calves and bullocks all around,,
A nAddehed cow frdm out the main nihb flew *
And horrted poor Playboy,Mettirig daylight through.
Nonbah has of its best-horse beeri bereft
And now there’s nothing but his carcase left; -
Console yourselves and dry away those tears
For was not poor old Playboy full, pi years ?
He’d lived through many a hard long year of drought
And many a thousand angry beasts “cutout”
O ! Playboy ! thou art not’the horse of yore
For you will draft and jump again no more.

My Dear Moorabin da,
Anecdotelets of the Gaiety' Co.’s last night in Sydney
savour rather of the sadly retrospective, but I can’t help
remembering the funny figure the Gaiety giantess cut
when the pack of laundried youths who call themselves
the “ red-necktie brigade,” threw her their buttonholers
and implored her to pick ’em up. The Amazon in scarlet
lights couldn’t get at them to save her life, so the flowers
remained where they lit, amid much mirth. And, talking
Gaiety girl, I hear that one of them charmed the very soul
of a Sydney legal Johnnie one evening after the play by
turning off the gas with her bare toes.
By the way, Courtneidge is a most ticklish person. You
need only crinkle five fingers in the air half a-vard off him
to reduce him to a state of wild hysteria. One night
Marion Hood made claws at him just for mischief, on the
stage, and the audience heard him beg her to leave off.
I wonder why the Australian gilded youth is so proof
against the (matrimonial) charms of the ballet? You
hardly ever hear of young Jurabuck leading Tottie to the
altar, as the peerage is so fond of doing. I, for one, can’t
remember anything of the sort —but then I am so young
and inexperienced, \ouhear of diamonds and drives to
Rock Lily or Heidelberg, but it’s a cold day when Flossie
gets a tangible wedding-ring grip on the worldly assets
of Mr. Pure Merino. The Australian society man has a
perfect mania for marrying in his owm set, or a little
above it: he is quite as appreciative of his own connubial
value as is any English spinster.
The leason wiy the local 11 Johnnie” is so much more
caieful in these affairs than my Lord Dazzle may perhaps
be ttaced to the fact that Lord D. is very weary of being
exalted and longs to come down with a run, while Jum-
buck hasn t had quite enough of swell society to put
himself outside the boundary-fence for mere love of a
dancing-girl. Though it doesn’t always follow, by any
means, that alliance with the ballet involves social
ostracism. A man may force almost any sort of a wife on
to his friends if he’s only rich enough.
At hich brings up the story of how a well-known Aus-
tralian mining potentate secured a clear field to win the
wifely affections of the Hebe in a diggings’ hotel. She
had two business-like suitors, the embryo potentate and
the village blacksmith. Smithy seemed so full of running
(one can’t control sporting phrases at race-time) that the
other man got frightened at his chance and offered Vulcan
a bundle of shares in Broken Hill to go away and leave
him in possession. A 7 ulcan took the shares, which even-
tually gave him a snug independence. But perhaps the
lady went dirt-cheap even at that.
It’s a good thing that a certain well-advertised and
benevolent Australian millionaire is worth seven figures,
or else the one thing benevolent about him would pro-
bably be the walls of the asylum of that ilk. You see, he
counts himself the guide, philosopher, and friend of so
many charming little women. One of his fixed habits has
been to order champagne for “ everybody in the bar ” if
there happened to be a pretty face behind it that took his
fancy. And then he would go and settle the pretty face
in a bar of its own while he paid the piper. Not only one
spirituous charmer, mind you, but blocks of ’em, so that
when he sees “ Jane Smith ” over a corner pub. he can’t
tell for the life of him whether he paid for the sign or not 1
The top skimmers of a Southern city are fostering un-
flattering opinions of their Governor and his “ lady
principally his lady—and you can run the entire society
blockade without meeting with a woman who wants to
talk cf me and Lady Blank” since the socicty-c//m-
-aldermanic garden pawty. The “ best people ” view with
equanimity a gathering of sweeps even round theGuv’nah,
if the Guv’nah keeps the functions of smut and smartness
things sacredly apart. But to invite ’em all on one
common level, as if there wasn’t a pin to choose between
Mr. Alderman Grocery Jones and Miss de Darling Point,
was the unkindest cut of the season.
The proceedings of that garden-party wanted the Riot
Act read over them. The municipal heroes not only
brought their flushed and gratified female relatives in
wonderful millinery - they also brought their children and
th dr dogs—but even vice regal hospitality shied at Toby.
The “outsider” guests roamed all over the house, pene-
trating into the bedrooms even, until somebody uttered
the mystic monosyllable ‘ Tea,” and a Balaclava charge
was made for the tables. People snatched pastry off
plates as they were carried past. “ AVot! No korfy !”
said one lady, indignantly, in the same tone people use at
a badly-managed hotel; but the climax was reached when
two male \ isitors seized a whole cake and dived out into
the lawn, where they sat on the grass, divided the cak e
with a clasp-knife, anddevoured it between them !
From a Northern (N.S.W.) town comes a small and in-
dignant story (only the indignation is on the wrong side)
anent the behaviour of a bank-manager who objected to a
hotel hireling cutting the bread at the table. “Please don’t
cut the bread—your hands are dirty !” said he in dis-
gust as the servant pawed the staff of life, whereupon she
fled insulted, and mine host politely requested the
“ banker ”to go and board elsewhere. Which he did.
I wonder how long a community which thinks itself
civilised will put up with the public loaf—so general
throughout Australia —the monument of bread for every-
body to maul ? And not only the slice down on the
platter, which is the ordinary way of wresting a crusty
bit, and which is quite objectionable enough—l once saw
a man lift the thing bodily off the plate, clasp it to his
shirt-front, and saw chips off it towards his chin ! The
hygienic possibilities attached to eating what everybody
has handled would make sensational reading in a health-
pamphlet, to say nothing of the aesthetic aspect of the
case. Whj’’ don’t they give each person a separate roll—
or out the bread ere it comes to table?
Max O Hell, please listen ! The public loaf at the Aus-
tralian hotel table is as bad as are the public comb and
brush in the Australian hotel bedroom.
The Australian squattocracy (I speak, of course, about
the well-settled, railwayed regions) has developed quite a
mania for evening-dress, and for one “grazier” who has
his “ supper ” in a tweed-coat (here are ten “ squattahs”
who “dine at seving” in a miraculous state of starch.
On a lake run, out West, everybody gets into a boiled
shirt or a snip, as the sex may be, before sitting down to
dinner, even if the carte is only paddy-melon a la King
Billy and emu roti. But odd things happen to the man
who carries too much swallow-tail in the wilds of Aus-
tralia. For instance, they say it’s no uncommon sight to
see a form in a dress-suit bounding excitedly through a
bog, when something unexpected goes wrong on the run
after dinner.
Some men haven’t an ounce of tact, and I think one of
the very worst cases in point happened at a Sydney
registrar’s office the other day. A young and happy father
was supplying the thrilling statistics surrounding the
arrival of his “ first,” and the stolid clerk was putting on
the usual air of excessive lack of interest in the case. But
when it came to the mother’s maiden name, “ Eliza Jane
Smith," the quill-driver leapt into life. “You don’t
mean to tell me,” he carolled, joyously, “ that Eliza’s a
mamma ! Just think of. that now ! Oh, I knew Lizzie
awfully well in the old times! You just ask her, will you?
if she remembers Gussie Sniffkins and moonlight ’’—but
the proud papa had stood about as much as he could, and
the rest of Gussie’s joyous recollections were lost in the
scuffle.
There is very little reverence for employers in Australia,
perhaps because servants are so genealogically inquisitive
as a rule. Somebody tells me of overhearing two of her
hirelings commenting upon a departing lady visitor w'ho
had been calling upon her and was being borne away
lolling stylishly in her landau. “ Wonnerful, ain’t it ?”
said Jeames to John, “ ’ow the butcher’s daughter picks
it hall hup I”
A rather amusing instance of some people’s boundless
self-confidence occurred at a recent vice-regal garden-
part}. Mrs. Captain Gingah had prevailed upon the
Captain to escort her to the f§te, and he not being a
sociable hub. his wife was quite elated at having him in
tow. So in conversation with Lady Jersey she said
effusively: “ Oh, dear Lady Jersey, my husband has come
with me to-day, for a wonder, because he never goes to
garden-parties ! ” “ Indeed ! Then lam honoured,”
said M.E.J. in impenetrable tones, and would you believe
that Mrs. Captain G. tells the whole story with quite a
little flush of pride? Irony mustn’t be too fine in Aus-
tralia, evidently.
Wet weather is responsible for a tremendous lot of
sentiment, from the wild flutterings caused by a pretty
ankle getting over a drain to the two-under-one-gamp
act, which latter takes a lot of beating. The connubially-
minded young man who goes out without an umbrella on
a wet day, and the young woman who goes with one
under the same damp circs, needn’t fear going bald from
excess of intelligence. In support of this, a Sydney daily
paper “ agony ad.” fits in nicely here—
W IL £ Lady who sheltered under verandah,
Oxford Hotel, King-st., on Wednesday evening
about 8.15, communicate with the “Stranger,” Daily
Telegraph office. Intentions strictly honourable.
You see, the girl did her part of the business all right-
charging over the mud for an eligible shelter with an
agonised upward squint at her best hat, but the honour-
able stranger on the doorstep hadn’t a parasol with him,
so the scheme didn’t work. If he had had an “uni-
brellar " how different it all might have been !
I am in receipt of a pale-blue song decorated with a
bevy cf robust girls and entitled “Australia,” words and
music by L. A. Pogonowski. It is in A major and written
for the middle voice, but its poetry is too depressing to
ensure it a run as our national war-roar. After waking
up Australia’s sons (w'ho are always asleep in the patriotic
lilt), the poet evolves the pessimism that “ when other
nations have decayed we vig’rous then will be.” There
are a good many robust nations over the road, all well
and hearty last cable, so Australia is meant to be a bottled-
up continent for a considerable period, according to Mr.
Pogonowski.
Little girls make apt mistakes sometimes. Mrs. Dash
had got a new governess to whom she was relating her
“ experiences ” at lunch one day, when litti ■ eight-year-
old leant across the table during a pause and si,i 1 to the
; “ Won t you tell us some of your experi
ments of life, Miss Smith ? ”
An amusingly Frenchy comedy-incident occurred not so
long ago in a southern city to a blonde legal man, very
much gpris with a young and designing matron who tried
to play the old trick of letting hubby get home at a
critical juncture to blackmail the gay Lothario.
It was all be-yootifully arranged. He was to visit her
at night, and she was to be blissfully alone. The pro-
gramme was kept, too, apparently, only the lady left the
room for “an instant,” and the Lothario heard strange
whisperings going on in the hall. Peeping over the ban-
isters, he saw his inamorata in animated sotto-voee with
the cupboard under the stairs, and he grasped the situa-
tion at once. Hubby was in that cupboard.
When the angel returned, her victim, after a wily
interval, excused himself, and reconnoitring the cupboard
observed the key on the outside. So he gently, but
firmly, looked it, put the key in his pocket, and returned
to his allegiance. Whether the husband ever got out, or
whether he was a modern edition of the “Mistletoe
Bough” heroine, doesn’t transpire, but the legal man
keeps the key (now gilded) to this moment as a memento
of what may happen to a man in Australia.
When Shaksper (it’s not clever to spell his name like-
anybody else) wrote about the urchin trudging unwillingly
to school, he couldn’t have meant a Queensland school,
for the unwillingness would have been natural under the
circumstances. A Brisbane public-school girl died the
other day from the effects of a blow, and her father stated
m court that “ a male school-teacher struck the child on
the head, contrary to instructions.'’ I should hope so.
Is the Brisbane parent in the habit of “instructing ” the
dominie to correct his olive twigs on the place where the
wool ought to grow? Or does the dominie spank them on
the skull as a general principle, which may be altered on
the guardian expressing a wish to have Johnnie tanned
lower down ?
A “anhas just returned who went ’Ome with the
celebrated society dame who created such a stir at one of
the Government Houses. Restates that the lovely one
made things hum on the voyage, and that officers and male
passengers had a high old time ; and he wouldn’t swear
that the Lascars hadn’t either.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
