■ A, m * programme, which consists prin-
cipally of the versatile John Gourlay, occupies
Sydney Garrick this week. This copious enter-
tainment is not, strictly speaking, a comedy, nor
a variety show, nor a nigger production; but it
embodies one comedy-drama, one howlsome farce
and a concert and some melodrama and ballet in
between. “Milky White,” the first item on the bill,
was started on Saturday night amid unbounded
enthusiasm, which was still enthusiastic but not
quite so unbounded towards the end. What
makes the audience cool off towards the end
is the fact that “Milky White” is one of
those old English dramas in which all the charac-
ters join hands at the end and precipitate them-
selves towards the footlights, where they stand in
a row—including the widow and the lout —while
the reformed cowkeeper delivers a few moral
observations to the audience. Apart from that,
there is no ground for objection. Mr. Gourlay
strikes a responsive chord all through as the most
mercurial and cantankerous little man who ever
threw his daughter into the street from the high-
est motives. Also, he demonstrates that it is
sharper than a serpent’s tooth to live in a parlour
which has walls on three sides only, and on the
fourth side a whitewashed fence, so that the cows
can put their heads through the opening and
get their horns entangled in the piano.
Mr. George Walton and Miss Amy Gourlay are
the other two praiseworthy features in the piece,
and the latter lifts up a sweet little voice in song,
only there is hardly enough of the voice for her
purpose, and faints in quite a new and much im-
proved attitude. Later on Mr. Gourlay runs a
successful melodrama all by himself, being got up
as the devilish villain in black on one side of him,
and the shrinking, golden-haired maiden in pale-
blue on the other; and does a turn as a robustious
and leggy ballet-girl, the awful thuds of whose
extremities are suggestive of a she-fiend breaking
cocoa-nuts with her feet. Somebody ought to lead
Mr. Gourlay gently aside by the hair and urge bun
to leave out this ballet-girl.
* * m
The “Living Models,” which made Satur-
day’s audience wear its umbrella to a stump in
applause, is & fare© b&sed on the wogs of two
snuffling old men, who, having gone into devious
ways in search of strange girls, are reduced to
get into a Roman toga apiece, and pass themselves
off as two unfinished statues ; while their deceived
wives remark that their legs are out of drawing,
and the daughter of the more unfinished of the
two statues, disguised in bov’s clothes, throws
water at her unnatural parent. The hand of Fate
also drives the wives into some species of classic
costume, which exhibits their shoulders in a reck-
less fashion, and causes a bust with a wreath on
its head to use bad language, and a lay figure to
protest in broad Scotch. Eventually, the principal
statue, in a Roman toga and a bell-topper, organ-
ises a rush for the door, and being captured on the
threshold something of an undefined but horrible
character happens to him, and he is led away
home. Messrs. Walton and Baxter—the latter
more particularly — make a lavish hit as
the two statues, and winsome Miss Bessie
Rignold, especially in her disguise as the perky
youth who cleans up, is a bright vision and a
gleam of sunshine generally. The Gourlay and
Walton combination gives material enough for
two shows at the same price, and there are only
two dull spots in the programme—one of the said
spots being a female who sings a distressing song
with the music clutched in both hands, and whose
endeavours to fix her vision on the song, and to
cast a witching glance at the gallery at the same
time, are painful to witness. No lady can sing
with one eye while she smiles at the gods with the
other; and in her efforts to do this she generally
loses her place in the music; besides, nobody seems
to know what the song is about.
-;K- * * *
Gleuloth’s win in the Melbourne Cup has
lent additional point to the moral of the “silver
King, which is that the horse is uncertain and
transitory here below. Mr. Rignold’s show con-
tinues to be a bright spot on the social horizon,
and if it doesn’t improve much it is because there
is very little room for improvement. Perhaps
Father Xmas grows a little better as time goes on •
it is hard to say. This ancient perjurer and house-
breaker is Mr. Shine’s greatest effort. He
looks just like the honest, retired seaman Who
looms up in so many picture books as the
superannuated secret of Britain’s greatness,
and his dirt, and his profoundly paternal
manner, and the haste with which he turns Queen’s
evidence, all help to increase the resemblance
Anyone who watches Ad. Scott’s British mariner
as he shivers his timbers in George street, Sydney
on a Saturday night, and then studies Father
Xmas, must recognise that the latter is just that
same manner grown old anfl disreputable and
flushed about the nose. Jaikes is as lachrymose
as ever, and this good but tearful old man is mora
depressing than all the wicked characters put
together. Wo would like to see the “Silver
King ” played, just for once, with a cheerful
Jaikes—if the faithful old servant of the stage
isn’t bound over in heavy recognisances to bo
melancholy at all times and under all circum-
stances.
* -* *
The fact that Britain s heir-apparent has
had a birthday this week doesn’t seem to
galvanise the Sydney music-halls and other
variety shows. The time was when the circum-
stance of Wales being twelve months older used
to shod a sort of radiance over the business and
lead managers to come out regardless of expense,
but these days are over. A few of them have put
on anew song or anew nigger, but that is about
all, and nothing short of Wales becoming 14<t
years of age all at once would make them come out
ni the gorgeous fashion of long ago. For all tins
results that are apparent, Wales might as well
give up having birthdays in this country.

William Rignold tells an Era man ; “ The Australian is
an enthusiastic playgoer. If one may say so, he is too
enthusiastic. If he likes a piece, he conies night after
night. He will come every night for a week, and then he
is satiated and must needs have something else. The
pro! lem of theatrical management out there is most
difficult, and has yet to be solved. The Australian will
not have a mean production. He must have everythina-
of the very best.” William complains that globe-trotters
are continually jerking in with—“ This is not as good as
it was in London,” and Australians become dissatisfied
What seems least popular in Australia is the • society ’
drama.” *
Despite hard times, Bland Holt has crowded houses
every night in Newcastle. He stops four weeks. He is
the only histnon who can profitably hold the boards in
Newcastle over a week A feature in his show is the
clever soling of Miss Blanche Read, a ten-year-old me
cocity of local growth whom Holt discovered and for
whom he predicts a great future.
Young Haydn Parry’s comic operetta, “Cigarette” is
catching on, as it deserves to do (writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent), for it is one of the most melodious
pieces of work heard for a longtime. Parry has Quite
caught the Offenhachian method, and his choruses and
concerted pieces are worthy of all praise. The book is
however, very poor, and quite unworthy of the author’,
Warham St. Leger, who is really a clever fellow. Geraldine
Ulmai now plays the leading part (screw, LBO a week)
and the Begum Amadi—formerly Miss Treherne, or some
such name, of (he Gaiety, who married some coffee-
coloured sw eh—is the second string. She sings and acts
admirably, she is know nin “ sassiety ” indifferently as
the Begum or (lie Princess ” Amadi.

Charlie Harding is going to be a hig-wig in the musical
world. He has been engaged in London by Lago (a very
keen judge) for a year for £4O a week, and after hearing
him practising several times Lago extended the engage-
ment for another year at double that salary. Harding will
probably play in “ Maritana,” “ The Bohemian Girl,” and
“The Magic Flute.”
As Madame Marches! predicted, Frances Saville’s
Juliette (at the Theatre de la Monnaie—Opera House—
Brussels) made an enormous hit. The beautifuj little
Australee woman is destined to shine brightly in the
operatic firmament.
The London cry is “ Still more music-halls.” The latest
proposal is to build a monster one in St. Martin’s-lane,
not far from the Empire—to capture the overflow of the
Alhambra and Empire.
“ The Queen of Manoa,” writes Tub Bulletin’s London
correspondent, by last mail, plays up much closer than it
did the first few nights, but it still leaves much to be
desired. It draws good houses, but people really go lo
see Mrs. Langtry’s gowns and diamonds, as they did to
“The Fringe of Society.” But, after all, the stock of
gobemouches is soon exhausted, and a new piece is
already in preparation to succeed “The Queen of Manoa.”
In this, the Langtry will play the part of a “business lady,”
which, as she is one of the keenest women in the world as
regards £ s. d., should suit her down to the ground.
The jewels she wears in one scene of “The Queen of
Manoa ” are said (for advertising purposes) to bo w irth
over £Bo,ooo—who suffered ?—and a detective is con-
stantly on guard at the stage-door.
Among the Meston-Puroell blacks is the King of Prince
of Wales Island. Purcell is teaching him baccarat, against
the time of his inevitable meeting with the other Wales.
Our London correspondent has some doubts whether
Lionel Rignold will make a hit in Australia. In the
“ English Rose ” he depicts to the life a character very
common in England, but which is probably unknown here.
Albert Chevalier was a frost at Manchester because they
didn’t understand New Cut coster lingo there.
A good tip in theatrical advertising is given by Jean
Middlemiss in her latest novel, “ How I became Eminent.”
The hero is a young actor, one Henry Yandeleur, run by
a Yankee, who circulates all sorts of pars, to the effect
that Yandeleur is a lunatic—followed, of course, by cer-
tificates from medical men that he has broken down hope-
lessly. Yandeleur insists, notwithstanding, upon his per-
fect sanity, and determines to play Hamlet at all hazards.
Curiosity'is aroused, the theatre is packed, and Yande-
leur makes a big boom.
Two Italian journalists, named Biasoli and Poggiani,
lately gave a benefit to the distressed poor of Verona, and
realised a large sum. The show consisted of the two
pressmen drinking a bottle of wine in a cage occupied
by five lions, and all Verona paid to see them eaten, and
was disappointed. A circus with four lions is about to
visit Sydney, and if Editor Curnow, of the S.M. Herald,
will K o into the cage in the good cause, Tub Bulletin
doesn’t mind sending a representative along with him.
Frank Thornton again invades Australia. Date yet
unfixed, but it will not be ere he has drawn a big cheque
out of Hawtrey. Not alone did H. break his contract,
but he put up Penley to play Rev. Robert Spalding in
“The Private Secretary,” which Frank naturally feels
was a serious professional reflection upon him.
About ten male and female aborigines were lately
recruited in and around Townsville district by Cunning-
ham, a Yankee showman, concerning whose alleged treat-
ment of his black charges on the voyage to’Frisco The
Bulletin lately printed a letter. Before taking them out
of the province, however, he was called upon by Queens-
land Government to provide a bond for £5OO, given by a
local man, ensuring the safe return of the party, which
included, by the way, a “Lady Jane” and the “ Hon.
William Queensland.” The Bulletin is informed that the
local tradesman who gave the bond in question has since
filed his insolvency petition. The bond was duly drawn
up and executed, and set out that should Cunningham
fail to return the blacks to the district, within five years,
then £5OO was to he paid to Government by the bonds-
man, unless (sarcasm!) good and sufficient reasons were
given for “failure herein." A winter residence amid the
snow of Chicago will probably furnish all necessary
reasons. But if that does not satisfy the Government,
how does it propose to get £5OO out of a liquidated
trader? This is rather a conundrum to the cognoscenti
concerning Northern liquidations.
Leamar will, after all, take a leading part in Williamson
and Musgrove’s Sydney panto., and take it well. The
little woman is an adept at pantomime “ go.”
Dust, pulmonary fatal dust, is said to account for so
many professionals being turned into that article at
Johannesburg, South Africa. These fatalities recall Ingle-
wood (Vic.) in the old times, when Miss Herbert, Graham,
and Webster fell victims there.
The events of Miss Emilia Woods’ over-crowded Sydney
Town Hall benefit were the giddy boom-de-ay bow, twice-
repeated, by a young lady vocalist in return for encores,
and the perching of a weeping small boy, with his hands
dug into his eyes, on a piano-stool while his fond parents
were requested to “come forward.” They did, but until
then most people thought the youngster was a ventrilo-
quial “ Master Billy ” figure.
Mr. Max Heuzenroeder and Mr. H. C. Evans, of Ade-
laide, are sawing-out an “Australian comic opera, with
plenty of local colour,” in which the bunyip figures.
Which recalls, in a roundabout way, a “comic” opera
produced at Government House, Sydney, a few years ago,
during the Oaringtonian regime, by two Scotchmen,
named Moser and Maclean. Mac.’s part, the music, which
comprised some very pretty airs, was all right enough,
but Moser’s humour—O, Moses ! The alleged satire was
directed against Australian muscle-worship, and one pare
of the dialogue (thrown, not at an audience of slaughter-
men, mind you, but at a fashionable, V-shaped lot of
people) ran “ Oh, what’s the use of this fellow—he’s as
narrow-chested as a curlew!” “But he has plenty of
pluck” “Yes, but what’s the use of pluck without
lights?” That was the only time The Bulletin ever
visited Government House—in the hope of seeing Moser
poll-axed. We have carefully avoided the beastly vulgar
place ever since.
Emetic from an Argus musical “criticism ” “In the
last number, embracing three items, the diversity and
plasticity of Madame Pabst’s style were exhibited in her
interpretation of the tripping music of Rameau’s gavotte,
of the quaintly pretty phrasing of Heller’s ‘Zur Guitarre,’
and of her husband’s musical poem, entitled ‘ Fairies in
the Moonlight,’ forming one section of his ‘Night Voices.’
It described, in the picturesque language of the instru-
ment, the shimmer of moonlight on the rippling waters of
a forest lake, the rustle of a faint breeze among the
hedges, and the fantastic movements of a throng of little
elfin creatures in the long grass and among the night-
blowing flowers. It was so sympathetically rendered that
it was followed by an enthusiastic encore, in response to
which Madame Pabsi played what might be called another
canto from the same poem, full of graceful fancies and
suggestions of images of old romance, such as are
called up when reading Hood’s ‘ Plea of the Midsummer
Fairies.’ ”
Dear Bulletin,— Your Melbourne dramatic critic in-
clines to think that Marian Mitchell is a “ recent star ” in
the juvenile opera constellation. Now, lam sure that
Sydneyites are not likely soon to forget the finish—equal
to that of Alice Rees—with which she sang “ Poor Wan-
dering One ” as Mabel, in the “ Pirates,” last December.
I don’t think she toured Maoriland with the company,
most of whom were polished old stagers when they
appeared in Sydney, and fancy that her first appearance
on any stage was as Nanki-Poo—which she fairly mur-
dered through nervousness—at the Criterion—Yours,
Habitue.

A recent success in London, “ La Statue du Com man-
deur,” is stolen from the French, of course. Plot con-
sists in a giddy and hiccupy johnny solemnly asking a
big, bronze statue to come home and sup with two pretty
girls; to his surprise the figure lumbers down from its
pedestal and comes. Itis the statue of a man who was very
thirsty when alive, and the idea of supper and two females
wakes up the dissipated image in an alarming fashion.
In act 2 it is drinking copiously and hugging the girls;
m act 3 a hornoly intoxicated statue, with its hat on one
side and its toga up about its ears, is prowhng through
th f streets looking for its pedestal and not find g it. It
fifets so mad at last over its ill-success that it stumps back
t,o the scene of revelry, and strangles its host as he s’eeps
amonfr the emp ty bottles and the victuals, and the cur-
tain comes down with the bronze gorgon sleepily winking
an( j no dding at the deceased, who sits in the opposite
chair and glares hack at him.

charged with murder has, in a ’Frisco court,

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Every horse to his trade! The owner of
Glenloth, winner of Melbourne Cup, run amidst rain, is
a milkman. The watery day therefore suited his beast.
# * *
Is it for nothing, ye punters, that “Glenloth
and “ Gone to Hell ” are composed of the same letters .

Chaffey Baker, M.L.C., the boss of Adelaide
National Ass, dreamt Glenloth for the Cup, and hacked
him for a heavy sum.
# • *
Glenloth was a restored invalid, and yet he won
over the heaviest going ever known. Even his trainer
had concluded he was no stayer.

£22,392 ; W. Australia, £9OOO ; Maoriland, £9OOO ; S. Aus-

Jimmy Ashworth, John Tait’s old jockey, died
last week at Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney, from the
effects of an accident at Warwick Farm. With him has
gone probably the last of the old-time jocks;
The Jewish Herald, Melb. complains of talk-
ing in Synagogue. “ Worshippers were even heard to
discuss the prospects of the different horses engaged in
certain races ; ” and elsewhere, “ Our race is in imminent
peril.” No fear. Their race always comes out on the
right side.
9 9 *
While waitingforCupnewsat Sydney Tattersall’s
each of 30 bookmakers and horsey-men (all in “ the
know ”)“ placed the horses.” Notone picked Glenloth
or Ronda for a place, and Penance was allotted third
place by one only. Yet all the savants knew it was rain-
ing in Melbourne!
* » *
Fifty thousand coupons were sent into a Mel-
bourne newspaper office to tip the Melbourne Cup first
three, but not one exactly struck them.
* # #
Glenloth, in about the biggest sweep, was
hawked in vain for £2OO, while Malvolio sold for £l5OO.
# * #
The stupidity of the Melbourne tote-punters!
Threepence in the shilling was annexed in the ordinary
way by some men who ran a machine on the flat at Caul-
field. It happened that tor one of the races on Cup
(Caulfield) Day there were eleven investors, who all bought
a shilling ticket for the same horse. The popular fancy
delighted the eleven idiots by winning in a canter, and
when they went up to collect their div. the tote yielded
them back 9d. apiece. They had each paid 3d. for the
privilege of spotting a dead-bird.
• * *
It is stated by those “in the know ” that N.S.
Wales contributes less money to big sweeps than either
Queensland or Victoria.
# • *
The Sydney totes pay an average of 40 per
cent, more than'the prices laid on the course on Melbourne
Cup day. After deducting 10 per cent, commission, and
the sum left for second and third horses, one gets an idea
of the rosy time the books, have had. And yet you hear
half-a-dozen of them cursing their bad luck, because they
each laid one wager of £33 to 1 against the winner.

Surely Wallace, Wilson, Hordern, and others,

* * *
Donald Wallace is a bit of a Scotchman in his
way. When Mentor won the Cup he paid Hickinbotham
his share of the stakes, according to agreement, less a
proportion of the cost of the entrance and acceptance
fees. Hick., however, got square with him, by holding
out for his share of the value of the £l5O prize.
9 9*
Since Tattersall first started his sweeps, a certain
Sydney solicitor has invested £2 in each one. His luck is
phenomenal: he has drawn one first horse, two seconds,
four thirds, several starters and non-starters.
• • •
When the late metallician, Bill Branch, was
doing business on Melbourne Cup at the Victoria Club,
Melb., a certain high postal official of another province
used to wire every day to him: “ Good morning, my dear
friend. Hope you are well. What do you think of So-
and-so’s chance ? Put a tenner on your fancy for me ;
also, a tenner on, &c., &c. Hope you have good win.”
But don’t suppose these messages were paid for. Oh, no;
they were sent as “official memos.,” and rushed through
at the expense of messages which were paid for.
* * *
The V.R.C. is fencing in the flat at Flemington
to keep out “ undesirable characters ” —one objectionable
tout in particular, who threatens to peg out a claim on
the track right opposite the grandstand. The club says
that it has no intention of charging for admission to the
flat, hut when the fence is up, it is ten to one they’ll go
for “ the gate.”
* * *
The Victorian Champion Fours race turned out
a fiasco, owing to all the boats fouling at the start, except
the Yarra Yarra, which crew jumped to the front and
finished leading by half-a-dozen lengths. The race was
re-rowed on Derby Day, when no accidents took place,
but with the result that the same crew won somewhat
more easily than before, in 18m. 12s. This is their third
year in succession.
# # *
Some titled London “ sports ” are subscribing
fora testimonial to Peter Jackson, for having thrashed
five roughs who insulted a barmaid. Had some con-
sumptive individual taken herpart and got knocked about
tor his trouble, they would probably have said it served
him right. The world greases the fat pig.
The Sydney morning papers have evidently
entered into a compact to boycott prize-fights. The boy-
cott, however, only extends to local prize-fights. The
most exciting match can take place in Australia without a
word being said about it in either the S.M. Herald or the
Daily Telegraph. But even the Herald prints a London
cablegram to the effect that Jim Hall and Fitzsimmons
are going to fight at New York for £5OOO, and that
Mitchell will also fight Corbett there for iOOOO. Every-
thing, even pugilistic news, is sacred, if it comes from
or via ’Orae.
# * *
Is there anything in that rumor that a Sydney
club, backed up by a wealthy patron, is trying to get
Lyons, the burly Adelaide cricketer, to leave for Sydney?
Jack Lyons has a temporary billet on Adelaide census
staff, which may shortly terminate, but his Adelaide
friends will not let him go without fighting.
• # #
The initial meeting of the N.S W. Trotting
Association is fixed for 16th inst., and in the interest of
that really noble animal, the trotting horse, it is to be
hoped an effort will be made to raise (he sport from that
slough of day and electric-light robbery into which it has
fallen. Under the old regime no effort was made to
cleanse the sport when opportunity offered. As an
instance, for months, aye years, a certain I ruse ran
nowhere and then he came out at the Driving Park one
day and starting from scratch fairly distanced his field,
yet his owner was not even cautioned. And there are a
dozen other cases equally bad. To meet these things the
Association should reserve the right to put up its own
rider and prove the speed of an animal.

A man was fined nnd heartily bullied in Mel-
bourne the other day, at (he instance Of a Society for the
prevention of cruelty to animals, or something of the
kind, for having “sooled ” his fox-terrier on to a cat.
The defendant hadn’t sense enough to insist that his deg
was a greyhound and the cat a hare.
* * *
At a recent W.A. dog-show, an exhibitor
entered two canines W'hich he believed were bull-terriers.
Anyhow’, they were full brother and sister, and of the
same age, and so he naturally entered them in the same
class. The bitch somehow possessed a long and tufted
tail, which failed to escape the eye of the judge, a tre-
mendously doggy man, who sagely remarked : “There is
a mistake heah. This is not a terriah ;itis a pointah,”
and straightway replaced the bull-terrier ticket with one
marked “pointer.” Result: First prize for the dog as
bull-terriah ; likewise?, first prize for the bitch as pointah.

m * *
The Otago Witness (M.L. ) reports that the
Dunedin Hunt Club has met with a misfortune. This
reckless crowd of Nimrods used to chase after a hare-skin
which was dragged over a rough country at the end of a
string. A tame kangaroo used to sleep on the hare-skin
to make it “high,” and therefore the institution called
itself a Kangaroo Club ; but the marsupial was shot, the
other day for trying to rip up its owner, and now, for
want of being slept on, the flavour has gone out of the
hare-skin, and the dogs won’t follow it any longer, and
the club is all broken up.

At Mentone (Vic.) races, Radoo won the
handicap, but the race was given to Submission, because
Radoo started in front of the post. Only two stewards
decided, inasmuch as the others had been betting, and
the Y.R.C. holds the dec sion invalid. Here is racing
w'ith a moral.

A few days before the Brisbane double-
hanging, the condamne Gleeson intimated to the
gaoler that he would like to give a “ last supper ”
to his fellow-hangee Moncado, and wrote out the
following luxurious mend ;
“Sucking-pig stuffed with pork-sausages, stuffing made
w'ith breadcrumbs, pepper, salt, thyme, sage, parsley,
butter, yolk of Iw'o eggs ; sauce, brains of calf or sheep,
flour, salt, pepper," parsley, butter. Entree.—Rump
steak, with walnut and poaohed-eggs. Vegetables.—
Green-peas carrots lib., turnips lib., beans Hb.,
cauliflower. Salad.—Cucumber, with boiled eggs. Boiled
cabinet-pudding.—l 2 eggs, lib. sultanas, lib. raising lib.
currants, candied lemon-peel, essence lemon. Plum-
nudding.—llb. suet, 1 dozen eggs, bottle rum, flour, 21b.
loaf bread, small packet baking-powder. Fruit.—Bananas,
oranges, pineapples, American apples. Lemonade, 6
bottles ; 61b. white loaf-sugar, 171 b. pound cake, 3 packets
cigarettes, 2 dozen cigars.”
The request was not complied with, probably
because the authorities suspected in it an attempt
to cheat the gallows by means of “suicide through
blowing-out.” Gleeson declared that, once the
banquet was off his mind ho intended to devote
the rest of his life “to prayer and mi dilation”
—not indigestion.
“JIMMY ASHWORTH.”
His last race is over, God send 'twas a u in
And that when he rides to the gate.
In the yellow and black, for the final “ weigh in,”
The clerk may declare Ashworth “ weight.”
And then when he reaches the place of the shades;
And dons the celestial garb,
I pray he may meet in elysian glades
Old John and the black-coated Barb. •
And further I hope, if they fancy such things
Where lightly on pinions they float,
They will leave poor old Jimmy minus lire wings
And give him instead a red coat.
So that when my last race is ridden and o’er
He’ll come as of old down the rails,
And jog by my side as he used to of yore
As I ride to the heavenly scales.

Mr. William Knox, secretary of- the Pro-
prietary mine and the,Mine Owners’ Association,
has the burly form, the hooked nose, the big black
moustache, and the general air of the gentleman
who cracks his whip in the centre of the ring, and
is the outward and visible authority of the circus.
And doesn t Knox crack his whip! He claims
the Earl of Ranfurly as the head of his family,
and has called his new house on the fringe of
lOorak after the family estate. He was originally
a bank clerk, then became a sharebroker, was
appointed secretary of the Proprietary mine, and
has gfown up with his place.
'.That, bitter-tongued, eccentric pessimist,
W. A. E. West-Erskine, M. A., the S.A. legisla-
tor, the news of whose deatli abroad has been,
cabled, was a dudish squatter, of the high-collar.
and mutton-chop-»hisker style of architecture.
To his personal friends he was a good-hearted
fellow, rather college-y and deucedly Conserva-
tive. He was a bachelor and woman-hater,
because —Well, no one ever suspected West-Erskine ’
of knowing how to .mash a girl; but for all that he
was a fop. Erskiue (his name was originally
West, but he changed it when a relative left him
a; fortune) was one of the first to tap Broken Hills,, '
and drew 'therefrom, when dividends flowed freely,
£(>000 a month. His recent utterances in America,
the financial state of Australia will be remem-
bered.
kir .fames Macßain, President of the Vic-
torian Council, died last Friday. He was a vera
solemn Scotch squatter, aged 64, and the most
impervious man in the matter of humour iq
all Australia. Among , other things he was a
Presbyterian lay dignitary, and a member of the
Association for the-Protection of Aborigines. In
1887, his unblemished dullness earned for him the
distortion of knighthood, and a year or two
previously his massive solemnity had gained him
the Presidency-of the Council, which office, in
default of anyone drearier than himself; he held;
to the day of his deatlf. His property mostly
goes to a nephew, and as the name of that hepfiew
is Smith Macßain, and he is studying for the Kirk,
there are sufficient guarantees that the solemnity
of the family will be duly kept up.'- But there
was a side to the nature of the Macßain of which
the ordinary public, perhaps, knew little, and what
it was is exemplified by the following anecdote.
Some years ago a* shabbily-dressed newspaper-
reporter suffering from' phthisis, professionally
attended a Presbyterian assembly meeting. His
cough attracted the attention of Mlacßain who
remarked that he ought to go to Queensland and
live awhile on the Downs. “ That’s all very well
Sir James,” replied the poor scribe, “but I can’t
get a Irving for myself and my wife on the Queens-
land Downs. Later in the day a letter from
Macßain was handed to the reporter—“ I will see
your employers and beg for a holiday for you. I
enclose cheque (j 650) and hope that bv resting
awhile in a warmer climate you will recover
strength.

who is now doing five years fo.r conspiracy, to

colleague, Parkes. All though thbmase Abigail’s'
defence was' that he was ignorant, unsuspicious,
and unacquainted with business generally, and
when his ancient ally entered the witness-box and,

John Mackay, the present har,bour-maste r
at Brisbane (Q.) is the man who first explored
the Mackay district, and he has the empty honour
of giving his name to the sugar city. A few
years ago the first MTlwraith Cabin'et awarded
him a grant of 1000 acres as a reward for his
services, but though the grant 'Was recommended
by a parliamentary committee, the Griffith
Government, which came, into office immediately
afterwards, bounced the* old man out of, it,
Griffith stating that a harbour-master’s billet was
sufficient reward. As a further gratuity, however,
he got rid of the shipping-master (salary .£3OO a
year) and gave his work to old Mackay without
any extra pay. When MTlwraith came back to
office ho had forgotten his former protege, and the
ancient pioneer’s land-grant vanished for good.
He who trusts a corpulent politician shall be for-
gotten, arid he who puts his faith in a bony states-
man shall be taken in.
Mud Carnival.
The Melbourne Cup has, for many years past,
been under the ghastly patronage of the Devil,
The sun was wont to issue an invitation to the
fleshly scene, and smile cordially upon the hollow
vanities of Flemington. Admitting the Low
Church doctrine that the Cup is sin, there was no
denying that it usually carried a thin veneer of
rapture, and was, in its own evil way, a gorgeous
success. The balmy breeze, whispered praises to
the glory of many milliners, and every young
woman in a new suit felt that it was good to be
there. Nature—which is seldom favourable to
parsons, somehow—did her level best to tempt
visitors to roll up again next time, and have
another “go” of gilded iniquity, a fresh dose of
false enjoyment, and a further plunge into the
good old “ vortex of dissipation.” Different
horses finished first past the post on these occa-
sions, and were hailed as heroes by the thoughtless
multitude, but, as a matter of fact, the Cup was
persistently .sneaked by the same animal. Nicko-
demus, an aged black horse, with a flattering tale
and very warm quarters, used to pull off the
event in a canter. This fiery beast always pitch-
forked himself into the handicap and ran for the
bookmakers.
* « *
All was mire and misery at this year’s Cup.
Twenty thousand women went down to the Car-
nival and groaned, whilst forty thousand men did
worse than that. We are a man ourself and speak
from experience.
THE DEPRESSION THE MOTHER OF INVENTION.
LAWYER (to vag.): “(What’s your business?”
VAG. “I’m the drunk’s friend, I knock about the streets of nights, and
when I sees a gent drunk I hawks him home.”
' In the old, allpririgtimes, one viewed the arrival
6f the giddy throng with a fair amount of satis-
faction. The sight of sassiety people spreading
themselves around in their best clothes, although
Hot exactly elevating, tends to soothe. Loafing
under an azure sky to stare at the advancing
squadrons of ladies who look nice (or think they do)
used to be a very good way of putting in the first
half-hour. Real happiness is contagious, and at
noon oh a fine Cup Day we could make certain of
catching whiffs of contentment from women whose
parasols/alone had secured them against any fear
of failure. !) There were parasols on view this year
—damp, dejected things that tried to keep
feathers from going out of curl and failed
miserably. It was raining when the trains “dis-
charged their living freights,” and the mud-
stained buggies and drags gave up their wished-
they-were-dead ’uns in the carriage paddock;
raining when luncheon time came ; raining when
the races were run ; raining ever and always to'the
bitter end. We can feel umbrella-water trickling
down our backs as we think about it. “Let us
have a lively Gup sketch,” was the instruction
given to the writer, and the individual in question
ought to be personally forwarded to the Bulletin
by post. We soon become a lively sketch.
* # *
This year’s Cup crowd was divided into two
great parties—the people determined to keep as
dry as possible, and the people anxious to get no
wetter than they could help. The former (mostly
females) filled every inch of space in seats and gang-
ways under cover of the Grand and Maribyrnong
Stands, and were so jammed together that their
identities seemed to be merged into masses. The
latter represented a moving panorama of umbrellas
and a monotonous array of tucked-up trousers.
Here and there, of course, a notable facfb could ba
detected, even in the Stand. Early in the day wa
saw Max O’Rell, in the corridor at the back, lean
ing over the partition to speak to a lady who had
found rest. Perhaps it was Madame Blouet, and
very likely her husband was saying, “ Quel
misere!” or something like that. As an intelligent
Frenchman, he couldn’t be expected to say much
else. The inevitable “ Teddy ” Lonnen, with fair
Alice Lethbridge in the vicinity, were also appar-
ent about this time. Other members of the same
company made themselves more conspicuous
throughout the show. Spiteful matrons
and rigidly proper spinsters said that the
Gaiety girls “carried on” a good deal in
spite of the weather —but these reflections
were unjust. The untrammelled darlings merely
played at pretending’ to enjoy themselves, and
they gave a thoroughly realistic performance
on the whole. Addie Conyers loomed large, and
Alice Leamar was decidedly busy in white shoes.
Many ladies had white shoes at the outset, and
got them duty by degrees, but there were others,
like Mrs. Rupert Clarke and her friend, Miss
Ohirnside, who came prepared for the worst, and
were subsequently not out of place in the “Bird-
cage.” At these, with “nothing to spoil,” the
rest of the sex began to glare angrily after a while.
They had taken a mean advantage of the rain, in
a sense, and the women robed in unavailing
splendour felt cross about it.
« • «
The vice-regal persons were driven down the
course amidst the falling dew, attended by
dripping Mounted Rifles and soaked orderlies.
The band, on the cold, dank grass, struck up
“God Save the Queen,” and tried to wake up
loyalty, yet without disguising the fact that the
elements had trickled into the National Anthem
and caused the wind instruments to splutter.
There was more than the ordinary lack of
impressiveness about the arrival of “the
party.” Lady Hopetoun is a feeble suc-
cessor to dear Lady Loch in the “ bless you,
my people ” line 'of business, and Lady Jersey
doesn’t get herself up to paralyse them. Jersey’s
hat, which couldn’t have been quite new when his
grandfather bequeathed it to him, looked none
the better for exposure to the drizzle, whilst the
braw Kintore had caught cold in his off eye.
Lady Hilda Brodrick, in vivid green and pink,
gave a streak of holiday appearance to the proces-
sion, and maintained an air of festivity even after
they had crossed the lawn and sought shelter in
the long private box, but the vice-regal show was
but a poor reminder of other days. We have seen,
ah ! we have seen as many as six Governors and
nearly as many wives assembled in that box at
one time. The various suites used to be mostly
left outside to shift for themselves, and altogether
it was a noble sight. On this occasion there was
plenty of room to spare for the low-spirited masher
captains and things in attendance. At the tail-
end of the box the Earl of Northesk popped up
once again but what a falling off was there 1
formerly, when Northesk was Lord Rosehill"
or some name like that, he would grin by the hour
together, but since he came into his coronet the
noble earl has turned ruminative, and all the sun-
shine has gone out of his large front teeth. These
great people seldom left their seats except for
refreshment. There was no promenading on the
Lawn, no ascension to the top of the Stand, as on
Herby Day, no anything for them except rain, and
chicken and champagne in a private room at the
back. Mutual sorrow seemed to draw them
closer together, however. We observed Lord
Hopetoun sitting on a stool beside his countess,
who looked down upon His Excellency in a
decidedly approving manner,
* # #
The social portion of the carnival being hope-
lessly gone bung we turned our thoughts to the
horses and began to look anxiously for men who
knew something. Duncan Gillies met our eye,
but he didn t seem like one who had a moral cer-
tainty up his sleeve. We set him down as an
extinct volcano wrestling with another umbrella,
and passed on. Other celebrated statesmen may
have been knocking around, but you couldn’t see
them for umbrellas. As public circumstances they
went for nothing on a day like this. They were
simply so many “brollies” moving about in the
humid atmosphere. By three o’clock, when Meg
had won the Kensington Stakes, the betting pad-
dock was fast being converted into soup. The
falling off in the crush of punters from the winning-
post to the scratehing-board, at the conclusion of
each race, was now very obvious. In Bravo’s
year one couldn t make the journey in less than
ten minutes; this year it was possible to slosh
over the thirty or forty yards with comparative
ease. Bookmakers were glad to accommodate
their customers, and even said “Thank you ” for
half-a-sovereign, in some cases,
* * #
Descending the awkward slope in front of the
Members Stand you had to be careful how you
sloshed. Well-appointed gentlemen fell into the
soup freely, and several lovely light overcoats
bore evidence of their owners’ sudden attachment
to the soil. A wild shriek from the Hill pro-
claimed that something had happened about this
time. Looking upward, we saw that six maidens,
having attempted to walk gingerly down a steep
place, were descending unexpectedly, after the
manner of little boys coming down the bannisters.
The rails at the bottom of the Hill arrested the
flight of these foolish virgins, and they picked
themselves before the awful disclosures had
gone too far. They must have been very muddy
somewhere, but their skirts fell back into position
and shrouded the matter in mystery. Neverthe-
less, we felt for those girls. On the terrace a
more serious accident occurred to a fashionable
costumiere, who missed her footing on one of the
slimy garden-seats, and struck the asphalte
heavily.
* * *
The fixing-up of the Cup horses for the coming
struggle was full of gloomy significance. The hoofs
of noble animals were being doctored with nails or
light plates, and trainers, assisting at these solemn
rites, shook their heads misgivingly. The air was
heavy with the sighs of owners who considered

SYDNEY.
that “ anything might win in such going. Thre
horses were specially noticeable for their sturdy
hind-quarters —Malvolio, Honda and Glenloth
but nobody perceived the finger of fate pointing
to the two latter rank outsiders. Walter Hicken-
botham, now looking after Titan in a stall hard
by, had tipped Glenloth for the Caulfield Cup, and
Walter is a good critic of other people’s stock.
But the Melbourne Cup, in his opinion, was a
one-horse race for Malvolio. Indeed, last year’s
winner looked as though he only wanted a fair
course and good luek to make him win the race by
six lengths. Most of the others were insignificant,
or common-seeming animals compared with Mal-
volio, G’Naroo, Camoola, The Admiral and Paris,
yet the Hebrews chuckled at the downpour, and
renewed their offeas to back the fee-ild.
» * *
Presently we were under an umbrella on the
margin of the Terrace, waiting to see the finish
for the Cup. The horses were at the post, and
the rain poured as it htyln’t poured till now.
Away up on the Hill there must have been fewer
people than usual, but in the great forest of black
mushrooms no break could be seen. The un-
covered stand below had seemingly been roofed in
with umbrellas, and looking across towards the
Flat one saw nothing but a leaden sky lowering
on more ginghams. The Grand Stand was redo-
lent of steaming clothes and muggy (appropriate
name) humanity, and men who had been strangely
attentive to their wives all day for fear of getting
wet, were glad to come forth into the storm and
swear at large. The start was delayed for fifteen
minutes, during which time the Lawn, the pride
of Byron Moore, became a deserted duck-pond,
and the last hopeof a fair-run race was washed away.
We sank into our sodden boots and moralised
on the ghastly absurdity of the situation.
Was this the Cup-day of which several graphic
descriptions had been set up in type beforehand
Was it for such a melancholy tune that a myriad
of otherwise thrifty matrons had compelled their
conjugal victims to pay the piper ? And the men,
the deluded punters who had backed favourites
“straight out,” or in “doubles,” who had worried
their poor brains to find out what ought to happen
—was it on this spludge through the slush that
they had staked a month’s wages? The “ clever ”
trainers, the patient schemers, who had waited
twelve months for this glorious opportunity, and
neglected a dozen chances of “making a bit ” in
the meantime—what did their artfulness look like
now? As foolish as Noah’s unbelieving friends
when the Deluge came?
• * *
At last, George Watson told his mob of
drenched mannikins to go, and in ten seconds
the same number of horses had the struggle for
.£lO,OOO to themselves. The remaining five-and-
twenty might as well have laid them down in the
dampness and gone to sleep, inasmuch as the
first lot of mud-scatterers were the only ones that
ever got a look at the promised “ land.” As they
all trampled through the morass in front of the
Grand Stand the supporters of the favourite
guessed that he was a goner anyway, and guessed
right for once. Malvolio had given the leaders
thirty yards’ start, and was seemingly making
arrangements to gallop wide on the outside,
another thirty yards further than the horses that
hung close to the rails. A little while and Port-
sea tumbled down, to the great relief of tipAera
who had sworn by him and were able to bemoan
the accident which alone prevented their prophecy
from coming off.
» * *
The rain, trickling down one’s field-glasses, made
it difficult to understand what was occurring
until a horse wallowed home in the muck all by
himself, and then a long broken sob of human
despair .proclaimed that Glenloth had come to
pass. Number 13, followed by 3!l and 25 were
hoisted on the board amidst a burst of sullen
silence, but anon a faint disturbance in tire dis-
tance, near the Stewards Stand, denoted that a
faithful band of storm-beaten Hebrews were
giving tongue to their bliss. The time-honoured
public demonstration in favour of the winner was
omitted. The vast multitude saw themselves all
at once in their plain, unvarnished wretchedness
and became very tired.
» * *
The first paroxysm of grief was slowly subsiding
when we met a friend who professed to be looking
for his wife. He wanted information concerning
her whereabouts, so together we trudged off to see
whether the missing lady was concealed in a beer-
bottle, and might be poured out to us by mistake
at sixpence a glass. Colonial lager beer is, m the
ordinary way, sixpence a bottle, but the V.K.G.
caterer dispenses it like physic in nobbler-glasses,
at a “ tanner ” a sip. Nine nobblers to the bottle
—sixpence for the beer and four shillings for the
man who sells it. After swallowing our lotion at
half a gulp we asked the barman to mention the
name of the benefactor who Had provided this
nourishment, that we might pray for him when
we went to bed. The barman said Skinner,
and without doubting his word we retired. It was
raining as before, but we waded into the
Birdcage and came across the victorious Gleu-
loth. The horse, considerably distressed, was
being rubbed down by an odd-looking little Irish-
man and a couple of unkempt boys, all of whom
seemed proud of their occupation and quite pie-
pared to keep on rubbing till the Crack of Doom.
There was only a small audience at this interest-
ing performance. The difference between the
rubbing down of a successful outsider and a
“popular fancy” was brought under our notice
with painful clearness. We admired Glenloth in
a feeble manner, but wouldn’t have minded much
if they rubbed him the wrong way. In point of
fact, we rather wished that all the horses, espe-
cially Glenloth, were dead and buried beside our-
self in a lonely grave.
* * *
We left the Birdcage, musing on the whips and
scorns of fortune, and walked back ankle-deep m
dirt towards the gales of egress from the Carnival.
Most people were going the same sad way.
There was no music to persuade us that we
were happy, and the very last pretence of
festivity had dropped out of the show. Even
the push for the homeward train was but a ghost
of its old self. Fourteen people wedged them-
selves wearily into our particular coinpartmenc
and remained pensive all the way to Spencer-st.
terminus. One man certainly broke the monotony
of the journey, but he didn’t break it much. This
solitary exception said “ Blimy ! ” and fell back
into his train of thought. Back in the streets of
Melbourne we were glad to notice a casual merry-
maker enjoying a seasonable joke. He had tucked
His trousers up to his knees, and his muddy legs
were telling the true tale of the Cup as he walked
along.

now at a bagnio in Lonsdale-street, Melbourne.

certain financial trouble finishes, for a big political
potentate and three perfect ladies were in the
picnic.

A prominent question tit S. A. elections
next year will be that of Bible-reading in State
schools. Some of the churches, usually at war
amongst themselves, are uniting in support of the
movement. The abolition of religious education
in schools was designed to kill Papistry, it
didn’t but it is cutting the throats of the other
sects, who now unavailingly seek to retrace their
steps.

receive double that sum, but, as usual, the
amount comes from persons with from £2OO to
£5OO a year. The rich are allowed to deduct their
“losses ” from their income-statement.
Sydney Daily Telegraph pretends to
favour payment of members, and has written
leaders in favour thereof, yet it prints a letter
from its Maoriland correspondent, stating that
the M.L. Council, in passing the Payment of
Members Bill, disgraced itself in public opinion 5
that it had failed to do its duty and protect the
public against the shameless seizure of £lO,OOO a
year ; that its failure “encourages the feeling that
the Council is useless.”
The British Australasian (London) re-
marks that, for the very first time in two years, a
steamer has left Australia for England without a
big consignment of gold or bullion on board.
N. S. Wales alone, in the year 1891, sent out
£2,300,000 in gold and silver more than she
received. This is rough on the Foreign-trade
theory that no money goes out of the country in
payment for imports, but that they are paid for
in kind, in consequence of which the inrush of
cheap labour goods only stimulates production and
makes the desert blossom as the rose.
Professor Tucker (Melb.) has given public
utterance to the results of his recent personal in-
vestigation into the working of Continental and
British Universities. The results are meagre-
mere, pitiful suggestions for slight administrative
changes. By some excesspif Conservative folly he
carefully avoided inspecting such noble institu-
tions as the Paris University where so many
students are yearly educated without payment of
fees. High fees and an inflexible, hidebound
entry-standard keep many an Australian youth of
parts from positions usually filled by monied
mediocrity.
Brisbane Worker analyses Queensland’s
Assembly ;
Since the collapse of the land-boom and the decline in
Mount Morgan scrip, parliamentjhas become a huge finan-
cial morgue. There are 62 tombstones, covering as many
financial corpses, sitting on its benches, andlO Ihingmen,
financially free. A.l the rest are “ bung," “ bust,
"crook,” or “stiff.” When one speaks of the hon. the
member for , the language is purely figurative. What
one really means is the hon. the stiff ’un representing a
particular overdraft in the bank tf — —. They are simply
financial mummies dancing as the wires are pulled in the
associated banks’ parlour.
Men of Queensland, this is as true as that you all
must die.
Sir George Grey considers that the Maori-
land Ministry committed a constitutional blunder
in referring the recent Council-nomination dis-
pute to the Imperial authorities. Had a Tory
Government been in power at Dawning street,
Earl Glasgow would have been upheld in his high-
handed refusal to endorse the Premier’s nomina-
tions. In a similar difficulty the Canadians
would not have troubled Downing-street. When
Lord Elgin was Governor-General of Canada, and
refused to be guided by bis Ministry, the people
of Montreal assembled at Government House and
proceeded to pull it to pieces and cart away the
wreck for firewood. Lord Elgin tied to Quebec,
and was glad to cave in to save another Govern-
ment House from being pulled down about his
ears.

That’s Just the point. Why didu 6 Dibbs tell

interfering in politics, which were altogether opt
of her line, and refuse to recognise her except in
her private capacity ? The man who only recog-
nises the political female when he can get some-
thing opt of her is a poor thing anyhow.

A few items from the women’s work
exhibit which N.S. Wales is sending to Chicago
Exhibition • “Knitted counterpane,” “open-work
silk stockings and socks,” “knitted shawl,”
■“gentleman’s socks,” “infant’s robe,” “dressed
doll,” “child’s chemise.” What an exhibit! And
just think of the diseased vanity of the woman
who picks her husband’s common, every-day socks
off the line in the back-yard, and sends them off
to the biggest exhibition the world has ever seen,
for all the nations of the earth to stare at.
# * *
An old-time N.S.W. printer relates that
when that great democrat Parkes imported to
Sydney a lot of butter-coloured trash from India
to take the place of his rebellions white comps.,
the latter, while finishing-up their last day’s work
at the Empire office, blackened their faces as a
welcome to the new staff. Parkes happened to
put his face into the composing-room and sustained
a severe shock from the spectacle. The Eurasians,
by the way, were not an economical success.
They had to be paid T 4 a week, and couldn’t earn
anything like their money, but they sailed round
with diamond rings, and so on, and afforded a
much more imposing spectacle than the whites
whom they had supplanted.
# # #
Dear Bulletin, —The “ confidence trick ”
is worked in many interesting ways. For in-
stance, wealthy newspaper proprietors and firms
find it a cheap and effective means of advertising
to offer premiums for competitive stories, pictures,
designs for almanacs, &c., and at the same time
secure valuable matter at little or no cost.
Recently a big Southern firm offered premiums
for designs. The result was a great success from
an advertising point of view. Designs rolled in,
and numerous paragraphs appeared in the press
announcing the fact. After several weeks’ delay,
competitors were informed, per a two-line par. in
the morning papers, that Mr. Blank had been
awarded first premium and Mr. Swank second.
On enquiry at the office, they were informed that
no exhibition of the designs would be made, and
that they wou'd not be allowed to ?"c the successful
design ! —A ktist. Mel bourne.

Leprosy has now fairly settled down to
stay in Australia. What a windfall it will be to
the story-writers and dramatists as a means of
breaking up happy homes and working off the
villains! And now Jim’s tale will be made 100
per cent, more mournful by the bush poets.
* # . *
A big stir has been made at N.S.W.
Health Office since the appearance of The
Bulletin’s first article on the Little Bay leper-
station. Premier Dibbs, in answer to a question
in Parliament last week, informed the House that
the last lepers removed to the lazaret had been
afflicted with the disease for three years. As
some medico or other, no doubt, attended the
patient, the question arises whether he is legally
responsible for having so long failed to report the
case? On the other hand, if the diagnostic sense
of this certificated practitioner is so poor that
after attending a leprous patient for, say,
a year, he does not recognise the nature of the
malady—the sooner he is dealt with by the
proper authorities the better it will be for
the health of the community. The contagious-
ness of leprosy being a moot point, stringent
regulations should guard the visits to the laza-
ret of medical men engaged in general practice.
A doctor who lately visited the lazaret appears to
be strongly of opinion that one woman confined
there as a leper is not a leper at all. Supposing
that the case in question is really an uncertain
one, should it be compelled to mix with the
undoubted lepers'! We do not credit the allegation
that a woman in an advanced state of disease uses
the same bath as the patient in question.
* * *
The secret of the extravagant praise
lavished on each “ great” Australasian daily in a
certain publication is that each journal supplies
its own description which is not paid for. The
magazine man ought to go a step further and
charge for inserting the articles.

In the S A. Militia there was an officer
and a gentleman who was also a money-lender.
He used to advance coin to the members of his
company and deduct the amount with big interest
from the pay which it was his duty to distribute.
He was politely asked to go on to the reserve-list
when his acuteness became known.

A certain Australasian Judge, sincedeceased,
had, on occasion, a preference for being driven by
cabmen who didn’t know him. Once, outside the
Supreme Court, he hailed a cab. “Do you know
me?” he asked the driver. “Yes ! Sir Blank,” was
the reply. Then, instead of getting in, the Judge
simply cleared his throat twice, scowled at the
Jehu, and trotted away up the street. The cabby
promptly hailed a friend in the same profession
who chanced to come along just then. “ Jack,
that old (adjective) Judge wouldn’t take my cab
because I said I knew him. Cut after him and
tell him you don’t know him from a blessed crow
and you’ll get the job.” The other cabman
galloped along and obtruded himself upon
the majesty of the law, and, being asked the
same question, replied that he didn’t remember
ever having seen his customer before, whereupon
the Bench tottered right in. Opposite a certain
house in a remote suburb the Bench got out again
and proceeded to feel in all his pockets for the
fare. “Have you got change?” he demanded.
“No, sir,” said the cabman, with a sudden
hope of getting the whole sovereign. “ H’m,
I’ve only a sovereign about me,” began the Bench,
feeling in his clothes again, “and ” “Oh,
it’s all right, Sir Blank,” said the cabby cheer-
fully, “ I’ll call round and get it from Lady Blank
in the morning.” Then the cab and the Bench
glared at each other for a moment. “ Here,” said

the latter at last with a wrathful grin, “you’d
better take the sovereign.”

r Rev. Alex. Marshall, of Melbourne, having
been caught attending a stage-play, feels called
upon to extend his patronage to the racecourse.
He supports the amusement, but hates the betting.
The pulpit may thunder. The wager is the alcohol
in the Cup, and when the Church has persuaded
humanity to be content with temperance drinks,
it may convert the man at the races from his evil
desire to chance a bit on a “ moral.”

gregational Union, the Sydney Morning Herald

there, Mister James? General Booth recently
expressed a similar opinion, with the proviso that
you must feed the surplus wives well and not beat
them.

# # *
In a thinly-peopled district away back in
Western N.S.W. there is a man with 16 grown-up
sons. Therefore the family represents 17 sure
votes, together with, perhaps, a dozen more which
may be raked in by their influence, and the local
member has to conciliate them proportionately.
Eleven of the sons are in the Government service,
and the other five mean to get there sooner or
later. Periodically the old man plods heavily into
Sydney and “lays” for the member at his club.
When the member sees his shock head and his
spacious boots in the distance he always sends
word that he is out, and takes to flight, but the
patient agriculturist follows him from the club to
his residence, and from his residence to Parlia-
ment House, and sits around on doorsteps till h e
gets him. Then he states his case, which generally
is that Larry or some other member of the family
wants to get into the railway service, or the police
force. Thereupon the member has to get Larry
into a Government billet or lose the support
of the whole clan. The other day he set
to work to create the twelfth public official
in that family, and had just got the trouble
over, as he thought, when the old man came back
and reported that they had decided not to let
Larry into the police-force because he had varicose
veins. “Well, what do you want me to do?”
said the harassed legislator. “I want yez to
stop him havin’ them veins, of course,” said the
father; “can’t ye bring up the subject in the
House?” The senator promised that he would,
and now he is drafting a bill to disfranchise every
citizen having varicose veins, and to make it a
capital offence to have more than eight Civil ser-
vants in one family. The worm has turned at
last. When the old man comes back to demand
Government jobs for the other four sons the poli-
tician’s servant has orders to grind his skull on the
doorstep. Also, the member is looking for a
Government billet himself, for if Larry doesn’t
got into the police force one statesman goes out at
next election for a dead certainty.
Advice to Young Men—Dr. Richards’ ad., p. 21.
The champion fly-catcher of Australia is a
judge. He is only happy in the summer; in the
winter, when flies are scarce, he wears a melan-
choly aspect, lilce Othello when his occupation was
gone. But in the hot weather he is happy. As
lie sits listening to the evidence, he reaches out
softly after flies, and picks them off the bench or
grabs them in the air. Ho lies in wait for flies
while he is summing up, and he can solve a pro-
found law point and pursue the fleeting insect at
the same time ; in fact, his mental alertness
appears to be much dependent on the pre-
sence of the insect. He sentenced a prisoner
to death once on a blazing January afternoon, after
he had caught flies softly and persistently all through
the trial and all through the summing-up.
Then the jury returned with its verdict,
and the judge, who had retired while the twelve
was considering the case, came back with a dead
fly between bis fingers and resinned his seat on the
bench. The*verdict was “guilty,” the prisoner
had nothing to say, and his Honor proceeded in a
very few words with the sentence. “Prisoner at
the bar.” lie said solemnly, and then he went sud-
denly for a fly that was standing on his official
robes, and caught it, “ yon have been found
guilty of wilful murder” . Here the majesty
of the Jaw made a long shot at a blue-bottle and
looked at the squashed insect thoughtfully. “And
it is now my duty to pass sentence upon you.” The
court, at this stage, threw away the blue-bottle.
“ The sentence of this court is that you be hanged
by the neck ” Then there was another hiatus
while the Bench dived after two flies, and caught
one and smashed the other one aganist 1 lie cushion.
till your body is dead and ” (leanina forward
impressively and raising the hand that*held the
dead insect) “may the Lord have mercy on your
soul.” And as he spoke, his Honor cast his hands
abroad impressively, and threw the deceased fly
straight at the prisoner, as if it was his soul that
he was throwing away. It doesn’t amount to
much in print, but if ever George Titheradge
plays the part of a judge, that dead-fly act would
bring down the house.

A huge 7ft. seal, seemingly sick or
wounded, was swimming close to the rocks under
Fort Scratchley, Newcastle, N.S.W. A gold-
bedizened officer from the fort, attended by an
orderly with a rifle, two revolvers and ammuni-
tion, came down to slay the monster. Sitting
down, he indicated pantomimically to the orderly
that he wanted his boots taken off. The servant
yanked them off. The seal was then about 20
yards from the edge of the rocks. Resting the
rifle on the orderly’s shoulder the officer took
careful aim and succeeded in missing the beast by
about two feet. Scared by the noise, it made a
break for the open briny. Shortly after it re-
turned, and for about an hour the gallant officer
and his orderly chased it up and down the rocks,
the former taking pots from the latter’s shoulder
whenever the seal came within 30 or 40 yards and
missing it every time with unvarying awkward-
ness. Discouraged by the ironical jibes of the
beachcombers, he abandoned the chase, and after
IN THE GODLY TIME OF THE BOOM.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Each leading man was a white-washed saint
In the Godly time of the Boom,
But the storm of adversity’s cracked the mini
Since the Godly time of the Boom ; "
And the flaws are showing out bold and bare
And the cloven hoofs and the tail are there ’
That hid themselves in an aspect fair
In the pious days of the Boom.
There was feasting and rioting galore
In the goodly time of the Boom,
And the rulers wasted the people’s store
In a joyous way in the Boom ;
And the Chosen Ones of the Christian ranks
Said piously, “ Let us give God thanks,”
As they scooped the rhino into their banks
In the Godly time of the Boom !
And they gathered it in from great and small—
’Twas their pious way in the Boom-
Old men, women, children—all,
They robbed in the time of the Boom ;
The champagne flowed like the crystal stream
Their life passed on in a golden dream, ’
Who then such a fearful smash could deem
Would wreck the “ Elected’s ” Boom.
And one more godly than all was there
In the pious time of the Boom,
Who led the Christian youths in prayer
In a saintly way in the Boom,
And scattered the money of others so free
In aid of the churches and charity,
That Her Gracious made him a C.M.G.,
In the bright hey-day of the Boom.
Now, many of those who knew him well,
In the godly time of the Boom,
Declare he should live in a prison cell
For the deeds he did in the Boom
For the wrong that he wrought to the fatherless
For bringing the widow to distress,
And steeping their souls in bitterness,
Through his cursbd greed in the Boom.
But our rulers shelter him under their wings—
They were brothers all in the Boom
And “Prosecutions are costly things,”
And “ We lost enough in the Boom.”
They dread the evidence brought to light
Would show themselves in a sorry plight,
Compelling some more to take their flight
From the land of the Busted Boom.
R.S.

The religious papers will, no doubt, shriek
over the depravity of a sumptuous dinner-party,
given by Hopetoun on ante-Cup Sunday,
# # *”
Several of thebigSydneyjevvellersand other
houses talk of closing up after Christmas on
account of the bad times. It is said by at least
one tradesman that a not inconsiderable section
of local society, if unwatched, would leave little
room for anybody to amass a fortune in the
jewellery business. Not very long ago two very
well-to-do ladies drove up to his place and, while
looking through some goods, were caught “ pinch-
ing.” The accusation aroused much wrath, which,
by threats, turned to contrition and the recovery
of the article, together with others taken on pre-
vious occasions. The amount allowed for “ miss-
ings ” in such establishments at stock-taking is
surprising.

established in connection with the Victorian
Customs. The necessity for this arises out of the
fact that no man can find a woman’s pocket.

To popularise his paper a Melbourne
manager gave his office-boy 500 specimen copies to
distribute at the races, on Cup-day. “I could
have got rid of another thousand,” said the
youngster next J morning. He had sold ’em to a
butcher.
# * #
The “Fireman Duke” of Sutherland is
dead. For long he has been a social nonentity,
for he treated his first wife, a charming woman,
extremely badly, and a month after her death
married a young widow whose name had been
considerably mixed up with his during his wife’s
lifetime. Is is expected that Stai'.Urd House will
once more become a distinguished social centre.
The present (very young) Du'chess is one of the
noble army of globe-trotters, and spent some time
in Australia. Everyone remembers the book in
which she spoke of a certain Sydney society
leader as “a handsome woman with some tall
daughters.” This celebrated sentence is supposed
to have been guilelessly evolved, for the Mar-
chioness of Stafford certainly never managed to
make anyone in Australia believe that she owned
brains.
London Lyric Club lias burst, and a late
proprietor (or joint ditto) is under committal
for forgery. He used Lord Londesborough’s name
to the tune of nearly £75,000, and a cousin of his
own, an officer, has been completely ruined. The
system of finance by forged bills has been going
on for years. Where the money went to no one
can tell. There wore 1100 members at .£lO 10s. a
year, and most of these paid an entrance fee of £2l
in addition. This, one would, have thought, would
have been enough to keep the club going. The
Lyric (says The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent) was a great haunt of Australians, who
thought themselves no end of swells in being
admitted to that semi-Bohemian circle. One
always met a queer lot there. The raison d'etre of
such clubs as these was to afford mummer-wor-
shippers an opportunity of meeting actors and
other people wliqin the law considers vagabonds,
and hob-nob with them, but since so many of the
“ upper crust ” have taken to the boards, and are
to be met in private houses, people no longer care
to pay a heavy yearly subscription for the privi-
lege of staring at them.

It was in a
compartment of
the Melbourne
Cup express and
everyone had set-
tled down for the
night. Suddenly
one man who had
been fast asleep
started up with a
scream. The fool
had been dream-
ing, and his fellow
passengers frown-
ed at him for dis-
turbing them.
Next instant the
ladies shrieked
simultaneously,
and sprang upon
the seats. A “rat”
had run out of
the pocket of one
lady and climbed
upon her head.
The lady seized
the animal and
held it out at arm’s
length. It coiled
its tail around
her forefinger and
suspended itself
head - downwards.
It was a small
ring-tail opossum.
“It is my chief
pet,” said its
owner, “and I
always carry it in
my pocket.”

A Brisbane (Q.) firm, the other day,
advertised for “a boy.” Over a hundred appli-
cants—from youths up to grayheaded men—
turned up. It was loudly announced that the
wages were only 15s. a week, but only a few of the
applicants moved away.
* * #
In Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall, last Saturday,
a native Indian missionary told an audience that,
besides preaching to the heathen in Poonah, he
dispensed physio, “ thus getting still nearer their
hearts.” This may serve with some people, but
the missionary does not live who could win our
affections with a dose of castor-oil.

There is a blind beggar in Sydney who
knows his way about in more respects than one.
Asa rule, be keeps the sound optic shut, but
when particularly drunk he winks this other eye,
and occasionally reads the paper in a long-beer
bar. Some time ago a man tried to pick his
pocket, blit the beggar detected him and gave him
in charge. The magistrate, on sentencing the
accused, told him that one who was able to earn
his living should be ashamed to rob another who
had lost his sight.
Dr. Christian Endeavour Clark: “I like
the moral tone of the leading Australian news-
papers.” Naturally. “The leading Australian
newspapers ” write especially for Clark and
advertisers.
Dear Bulletin,—If a snake be held up
by tiie tail, has it the power to turn upwards as
far as the hand, so as to bite? An answer in the
negative meets with almost universal incredulity.
I venture the opinion, based on a personal trial,
that the reptile can only curl upwards a few
inches.—Yours, The Orphan. Perhaps some
correspondent will reply. We have never held up
a snake which hadn’t a broken back.
They never allowed travellers to stay at
the station, and seldom gave them rations. A sun-
downer sauntered up to the boss one evening.
Traveller: “Good evening. May I stop to-
night?” Boss : “ Certainly not.” Traveller goes to
hut, throws down swag, and says boss told him he
could stop that night. After breakfast traveller
goes up to the house. Boss; “What, you here
yet! ” Traveller : “Yes ; I asked you if I could
stay the night, and you replied ‘certainly not,’ ”
Boss; “Well?” Traveller: “ My name is Nott.”
Paris, the cable tells us, has outdone
Whitechapel in mutilative crime. The body of a
woman has been there found in an unfinished
building, cut into 12 pieces. Imagination here
stands aghast. There may be extenuating circum-
stances. The murdered woman may have married
her daughter to the murderer. She may have
inveigled him into her house and fed him on
home-made pie. She may have been his wife,
and endowed with conversational powers of no
mean order. Yet even a talkative wife should not
be cut into more than two pieces.
S. E. Lees, M.P. for Nepean, N.S.W.,
disturbed a Springwood picnic of wood-trade men
recently. Sammy, who is an aggressive and un-
compromising teetotaller, heard somebody “ hoo-
ray ing” his approach, and incautiously tendered
his hand to the foremost of a hilarious crowd of
bacchanals, who, thereupon, hung upon his neck
and breathed second-hand beer-fumes into his
nostrils, and tore his collar and trampled upon his
immaculate hard-hitter, and generally effaced his
body from the landscape for a time—all in pure
affection and untamed joy for their beloved
member.
Sympathy is one of the noblest and most
characteristic of the human passions. Who has
not heard the expression, “ It makes my heart
bleed to hear a recital of that woman’s wrongs or
this man’s suffering?” Sympathy is a natural
outcome of a pure, good and benevolent spirit; it
is an offering which the most lowly can pay to the
tribulations of their suffering fellow beings, which
the most exalted and wealthy can bestow on their
equals and peers in the life of this nether world.
A most wonderful sympathetic influence exists
between the human brain and all parts of the
physical being. All the nerves centre in the
brain, and no part of the physical organism can be
affected or disorganised without immediate know-
ledge being carried to the brain - the centre of the
spiritual being—immediately; not the finest point
of the finest needle can be applied to any part of
the body without the brain immediately becoming
cognisant of the fact, so perfect and wonderful is
the telegraph-like connection of every part of the
body with the brain. The nerves oovpr the entire
surface and ramify into every point of the
structure; a disordered liver or stomach causes
headache; rheumatism makes pain appear at the
affected part; so does neuralgia, toothache, &c.
The pain is only felt in the brain, though the sen-
sation appears to be in the part that is affected.
This is becaaise sensations are only experienced at
the extremities of the sensory nerves, and by a
wonderful provision of nature enables us to locate
the position of any unnatural condition of any
organ or function. Wherever there is pain there
is a chance of disease, and one cannot be too care-
ful in giving immediate attention to such matters.
A vigorous treatment should commence with
Clements Tonic. This remedy will cause all
members of the body to resume their natural
work ; it will assist digestion, assimilation, secre-
tion and excretion ; will restore sound and vigor-
ous action to all organs and tissues. This is
proved in the many cases which have been treated
with this wonderful remedy. Mrs. M. Huckle,
nurse, Sale, writes:—“For the last 25 years I
have been a martyr to indigestion and severe
attacks of biliousness, accompanied by acute pains
between the shoulders and in the left side. At
times I have been reduced to such a state of weak-
ness that I have felt as if death would have been
a relief. _ For years I consulted doctors, but with-
out receiving any benefit. I had taken so many
medicines without relief that at last I had lost all
hope of ever regaining my health. A lady friend
of mine persuaded me, at last, to give Clements
Tonic a trial. I did so, and I can say with truth
that the result was almost magical. I am now
better than I have been for the last 20 years. I
am only too happy to recommend Clements Tonic
to all who have suffered like myself. As I am well
known in Sale, you are at liberty to make what-
ever use you like of this, and I hope it may be the
, means of bringing relief to others who have
suffered as I have done.” Miss E. Sayer, Rose-
dale, Victoria, writes; —“I have suffered for
several years from biliousness and severe headaches,
and have tried several remedies without getting
any better. Hearing of Clements Tonic from a
friend, I was induced to try one bottle, which did
me a lot of good. I am now faking the second
bottle, and can safely say that I feel greatly
benefited, having recovered rny appetite and lost
the severe bilious attacks.”

Here’s a health to daring ventures ! Here’s success
to ev’ry boom !
Here’s a health to bogus bankers ! May the syn-
dicator bloom !
Here a healtli to cent-per-centers ! Here’s a bless-
ing on their name !
For in all their little troubles there is nobody to
blame.
If you bo a bank-director and vour institution
fail,
Should your balance-sheet be doctored, should
you qhanee to go to gaol,
Just inform the trusting widows when they make
their little claim,
In such terrible disasters there is nobody to
blame.
Should you chance to lay your dollars on a duffer
for the Cup,
Should you find j ourself a ruin when you come to
settle up;
Should your family be stranded, should your
neighbours murmur, “ Shame !”
Just inform them —in such troubles there is nobody
to blame.
Here a health to corner ’lotments ! Here’s success
to wild-cat scrip !
Here’s a health to ev’ry honest soul who gives an
honest tip !
. Here’s a health to all financiers in distress —and
, here’s the same
To all the little innocents who never are to blame.
Should your penchant for the bottle and the ballet
suck you dry,
Should your purse be quickly waning, while your
bills are mounting high,
It will soothe you when old Shylock puts the
cloture on your game,
To reflect that, in such sad affairs, there’s nobody
to blame.
Should your wife obtain an inkling of your doings
at the club,
Of your time and substance wasted on the turf and
in the pub. ;
Should she raise her voice in protest, just inform
the angry dame
In such scandalous proceedings there is nobody to
blame.
Here’s a health to dear old poker ! Here’s a glass
to rouge-et-noir !
Here’s a health to lovely thimblerig and royal
baccarat !
For, beside such fierce allurements, friend, all
other joys are tame,
And, in case they leave you high and dry—why,
nobody’s to blame 1 •
P. LUFTIG.
THE WOMAN OF MY HEART.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The woman of my heart was first
The woman of my mind
I saw her in the flashing foam,
I heard her in the wind.
She kissed me in the ocean’s salt,
Her breath my senses stole ;
I looked into the starry vault •
Of heaven and saw her soul.
The woman of my heart is now
The world and all to me ;
The radiant heaven is her brow,
Her eyes—the dreaming sea.
The mystery of life is she,
Whose love fills boundless space ;
Eternal life shall bring to me
The love-light of her face.
DINGO B.
A ROAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne.
On the south, to my right hand,
A mountain rises from the land,
Forest-clad and tempest-fanned.
Up to northward stretches wide
The sea, that here and there is pied
With foam-flecks that a moment bide.
Beside the grey sea’s ancient bed
A rgad runs eastward, long and red,
By which two villages are wed.
To you, ’tis but a country road,
Where weary oxen draw their load;
To me, ’tis that to Love’s abode.
To me that long, red road is worth
More than all roads upon the earth,
Since first this world of ours had birth:
For on it, when the sky is bright
With stars that stud the dome of night,
I meet my love by pale moonlight.

The S.M. Herald advocates a coalition,
thinking it would kill the balancing power now
held by the N.S.W. Labour party. Wrong again.
If the Conservative leaders of Protectionists and
Freetraders can “sink the fiscal question ” so can
their Radical followers, and that trouble being
got rid of, both sections are absolved of allegiance
to either brand of fisealistn and its whilom leaders,
and are therefore free to join the now cross-bench
crowd in forming a new Opposition.

GNORANCE NOT BLISS.
The above illustration represents the person
who doesn’t believe there is a cure for neuralgia.
If a friend presumes to suggest that he should
“ bear it like a man,” and assures him it “ will be
all right by-and-bye,” he retails language which
out of respect for our readers, we will refrain from
re-producing. He dispenses scowls strong enough
to crack an enamelled dish. He hugs his face
with hot cloths and hates himself and everybody
—even his mother-in-law—and occasionally kicks
chairs, and butts his head against unoffending
walls. He declares that neuralgia is a thing to
read about, not to experience, and he means to
have his false teeth extracted to see if that won’t
stop it. All of which conduct is very foolish ;
for if he would only invest half-a-crown in a bottle
of St. Jacobs Oil, and apply it according to direc-
tions, the whole trouble would be at an end. In
most cases the first application will effect the
desired end, and in no case has it ever been known
to fail. It acts like magic in expelling rheumatism
and all bodily aches and pains.
