The Wild Cat Column.
The Broken Hill strike lias at last been declared
“off.” Now the blacklegs will get their inevitable
reward : the weak ones, of whom there are crowds, will he
run out to make room for the best of the strikers.
# * *
A Sydney man says he will, by way of a start,
shortly run a big train-load of chrome ore into the city
from a mine near Gundagai.
* * *
A week or two ago, just when the English press
was in the middle of its comments on the financial institu-
tions of Australia, the financial institutions of England
started to go broke in an alarming fashion. In less than
a month, eight or nine banks and the like have placidly
flickered out. The worst smash seems to be that of the
Lbn-ator Building Society; liabilities, as per cablegram,
£3,250,000; assets, £50,000; reserve fund, a sham and
soul-destroying lie; prospects, nothing in the £. The
Liberator was an amusing office in its time, and in its
latter days it used to borrow money from the Hebrews at
17 per cent., in order to keep the whited sepulchre afloat
a little longer. In consequence of these revelations The
Bulletin has cabled to the London Times that Australian
confidence in British credit is much shaken, and that
Britain ought to do something to set herself right before
the world. This cablegram, so far as we know, hasn’t
been published yet.
* * *
A Melbourne paper remarks that “no bank
whose depositors are sufficiently numerous and whose
premises are sufficiently restricted need ever fail.”
There is a great and solemn truth in that statement. It
a savings bank has 10,000 small depositors, and there is
very little room in the narrow space before the counter,
and there are only two tellers on duty, and they waste a
little time checking each bank-book before they_ pay out
so that only about 36 customers can get their money
in an hour, and 30 of these are bogus customers—a very
ancient and successful device—who merely keep drawing
out and paying in money to block the panic-stricken mul-
titude, then the run might last for a month and yet not
one-thirteenth of the crowd have got their deposits out,
and by that time the directors might all be in Central
America with the reserve-fund.
* # *
One of the Melbourne associated banks about
which there were ugly rumours last year, has recently
discharged some 20 clerks. These will probably he re-
placed by others at reduced screw. When Chinese ledger-
keepers and Kanaka tellers run these institution* the
colUr-and-cuff brigade will perhaps cease howling in the-
“ Cawse " of capital.
* *■ *
Purves, Q.C., Madden, Q.C., Higgins, Leon,
Isaacs, and all the great legal guns of Melbourne, have
been "retained,” in case of emergency, by several
busted financial ex-magnites who tremble at current
whispers. But the Crown has a first lien on the ser-
vices of the Q.C.’s.
# #
Melbourne Leader sarcasm : “ The Australian
Banking Co. s.'cms to have been a bank where
the wild time grew, for bills were kept running
so long as they' paid a big interest, and security—
that is to say, security of depositors—was the last thing
thought of. " One manager was charged with swindling
by the directors, and as ha incontinently bolted the en-
tire hoard pursued him down the street, leaving the bank
to take care of itself. That was the only period when it
was comparatively safe.”
# * *
The weekly reports issued by the “big ” men of
Sydney Stock E mhange are doleful reading. One speaks
of the” “volume of business being exceedingly small.”
Another “ that it is absolutely impossible to write any-
thing of a novel character this week because the markets
have been as dead as nails,” but consolingly t.d ls: “ when
the tim come i our friends and readers know we are on the
spot.” B th predict a big improvement; a third more
truthfully admits the cheerl-.se, hopeless outlook.
# * *
A big unsatisfied demand for shale having set
in, the shale-bummer is stalking abroad. Worthless shale
can bs found a'most anywhere in N.S.W., yet good
samples are so scarce that the N.S.W. Shale Co. have to
w toh their truck-loads at Darling Harbour. Beware of
the man who wants you to go into a shale-syndicate. We
heard of one who went into the country recently and
secured a block of shale land, and then wired to his city
confrere to send up some good samples of shale. They
were sent, and he returned with undeniable evidences of
his great shale discovery.
* * *
It is now alleged of the Yilgarn (W.A.)crush-
ings that they are made up of mighty big tons. We can
quite believe it. There are many Adelaide men in those
big mines, and, if they did not introduce the big ton idea
into N.S.W., it was ceitdnly not for want of practice.
# « *
Satisfactory that the stones sent to London
from the New England emerald mine have been pro-
nounced true emen 1 Is by Streeter, who says their chief
fault is their lightness of colour, but lie has no doubt
darker ones will he found at a greater depth. At one
time such an opinion would have floated any gem-mine.
Times have changed ; moreover, the Burmah gem-mine
which Streeter h - pod to float in London last year has not
made people’s fortune.
# * *
The cable states the acceptance by the Lull rig
Co. of the £1000 challenge issued by Parke and Lacy on
behalf of the Frue Vanner. It also mentions that the
conditions of the competition are to be posted shortly.
We again predict that these concentrators will never meet
in open competition —the match will break down on the
question of conditions.
' # * •
It is time the Last Call boomed again. Observe
the sensational character of the latest report: “ Four feet
added to depth of mine without change-commenced
cross cutting east at 38-foot level, three hands extending
eastern cross-cut from main shaft. And to think 10,000
sovereigns was paid for half of it, and that subsequently
this salted mine had a market-value of £400,000 ! Ye
gods ! . *
* * *
Shareholders complain to us of the manner in
which money is being wasted in the Last Call mine. The
directors, no doubt, frankly admit it, but say that they
have no alternative but to go on. In a case like this the
companies’ law should he sufficiently elastic to allow of
capital innocently subscribed to work a mining swindle
being placed, say, in one or more Tat.tersall s ±100,000
sweeps By that means there would be a chance of the
victimised subscribers getting some of their money back ;
there is certainly none working the mine. More than one
Last Call man has piteously enquired : “ Might not some-
thing rich yet be struck if they go deep enough ? The
Bulletin admits the “might” and “it.” Exactly the
same thing might occur in any man’s backyard. Even a
*salt«d” mine might turn out really rich.
# * *
The crushings of the True Blue mine, Oroydon,
continue as incomprehensible if not as reprehensible as
ever.
* * *
The second biggest mining man in Bendigo now,
next to Lansell, is Dr. Atkinson, who for more than a
quarter of a century has combined medicine with mining,
and the two with much matrimony.
# * •
According to the recently issued half-yearly
report of Mt. Stewart mine, the profit on working
amounted to £10,810, over £4000 of which has gone to
wipe off the debit balance. The directors say dividends
have been prevented by unforeseen accidents to plant and
excessive railway charges on coke and bullion ; but as
they cannot prevent one or control the other it is useless
to give these things as excuses. They might more reason-
ably have said that had the ore contained another ooz. of
silver per ton a big dividend would have been declaied.
The Co., however, is to be complimented upon the
economical management and the care and watchfulness
displayed by the directors ; it only remains to hope that
the great desideratum-richer, ore—will be smelted during
the current half year.
# * *
The United Bluey reefs mine is also at Stewart’s
Brook, another mismanaged concern in whi -h everything
has been condemned for losing the go'd instead of the reef
for not containing it in payable quantity. First a
battery, then a Huntingdon Mill. The last report says
that there a"e over two oz. per ton left in the tailings
although the average amount saved is only 10 dwts. They
even oont- n I that the tailings contain more gold than the
pyrites. Mines are judged by results, and those ot (he
United Bluey have been ignominious.
* # *
An effort to resurrect the Royal Standard mine,
Stewart Brook, N.S.W., is being made. It is offered on
the easiest possible terms—no promoters’ shares, no cash,
and the mine, plant, and tools thrown in for threepence
per share application and allotment money. Some years
ago, the mine paid seven monthly dividends, and shares,
7s. 6d. paid, reached 275. Gd. To drain the mine a tunnel
was put in 750 feet at the base of hire mountain, and, as
might have been expected, in so small a reef it had no
effect. The shoot of gold is contained in an 8-in. reef,
and is about GO feet in length. Had the tunnel been
driven 150 feet instead of 350 below the old workings, or
the mine worked and drained through a shaft, it would
have been working to-day. The madness of driving this
long tunnel killed the show.
* * *
Two hand-drilling contests are recorded in
Montana, America. Two miners are said to have bored
38in. in a quarter of an hour ; two others 33 inches, both
in hard granite rock! If the rock was hard the time is
false, and if the time is true the rook wasn’t hard. We
have seen some of the best of Australian hammermen
but we never met two men who could bore 3ft. of hard
granite in a quarter of an hour. And we never shall. It
takes a rock-drill, worked by compressed air, all its time
to do this. It would take two good men at least two
hours to bore three hard feet in the ordinary way, and no
amount of desperate belting would enable them to do it
in one-eighth of that time. 3ft. 2in. hard granite in 15
minutes! The Cousin Jack “ upper ” man should smile.
• # *
Agents of the different refractory-ore processes
are issuing nonsensical challenges to each other. One
man threatens a £IO,OOO wager as soon as his machinery
arrives. For the same reason they will never meet—the
conditions that might suit one might be disadvantageous
to another. Australia is being inundated with new and
wonderful gold-saving inventions. The everlasting din of
“ refractory ores,” “ gold lost in the tailings,” has had its
effect. The great want is gold in the ore.
# # •
The Bourke-sfc. block upon which the Melbourne
Y.M.C.A. Hall stands was bought, at hoora-time, from
Matthew Davies. It would be interesting to know what
Matthew gave for the land, and what the Young Men
paid, so that we might appreciate the sacrifice the apostle
Matthew made for the “caws.”
# # #
In N.S.W. gold has been found in almost every
conceivable form of matrix. So many surprises have
occurred and so many well-worn theories upended that
we are quite prepared to remain unperturbed when a man
comes along with the discovery of a combined coal and
gold-mine. At Pambula we have a field so puzzling that
it is impossible to tell where the lode begins or _ the
country rook ends. Usually when the rook is barren it is
described as “country,” and when not, as ore. Tunnels
have been driven for the “lode” which has been passed
unobserved. The theory that quartz was the only matrix
for gold has been shattered, particularly by Mount
Morgan, Nevertheless, it is the main matrix to which
all others are exceptions’ to the rule—however bril-
liant these exceptions may occasionally be. Some-
times gold is treacherous and erratic in the best_ of
defined quartz fissures, but it is very seldom anything
else in any other matrix. In the great truly-formed and
permanent soddle-backs of Bendigo and the rapidly under-
laying quartz fissures of Charters Towers, gold has an
abiding-place to the greatest depth yet reached, and there
is no reason to show why it should not be quite as rich at
any workable depth. To the comparative absence of these
true fissures in N.S.W. may be attributed the palpable
difference in reef gold yields. Where in any part of
N.S.W. can be seen similar reefs ?
# * *
Pelton water-wheels are largely used in the
Yankee mines, because of the abundance of water avail-
able, and they might often be used to great advantage in
Australia. In N.S.W. any kind of water wheel is seldom
used, unless the water is right up against the mine, and
often not then. Boilers and engines we have seen fixed
beside a heavy, perpetual-running stream, the owners
being apparently blissfully ignorant of the existence of
such a thing as water-power. In America, companies
often combine for the purpose, and bring in enough power
for all. About £20,000 would have provided at Hillgrove,
water and electrical power sufficient for all the local
mines, and more than that amount would have, by this
time, been saved in firewood, which is always increasing
in cost, to say nothing of the cost and maintenance of
engines and boilers. As this is a slow country, perhaps
electrical power will be brought in when the best parts of
the mines are worked out.
* * *
A N.S.W. insolvent, the other day, stated,
amongst the causes of his bankruptcy—want of proper
management; making bad “deals”; fire; sickness;
gambling and drinking! Candid.
# * *
Few mining managers can resist the temptation
to “ pick the eyes out ot a mine.” On most fields there
are mines wherein enormous bodies of ore remain virtually
improspected because they will not pay (dividends ?) to
take them out. And yet in former days, in many of these
same claims, fabulously rich shoots of go and were found.
No thought of blending the ores occurred to these “ sky-
rocket ” bosses. An exception must be made of Bendigo’s
champion mine, the Garden Gulf}' United, when, under
the management of John Keam, whose aim it was to order
for the advantage ot shareholder and workman alike.
On a capital of £21,600 some 13 tons of gold were
won from the claim, over a million was distributed
in dividends, and nearly three quarters of a million
paid in wages, &c. Enormous bodies of the larger
lodes no more than paid expenses, yet all the ore
was taken out, simply by Ream’s judicious management.
There were veins in the mine described as "gold hanging
together by quartz,” and had Keam had these picked out
and crushed separately, be could have thrown into the
shade very often any yield of gold from any single crush-
ing ever reported from an Australian field ; but the life of
the mine would have been short and merry. The usual
fortnightly crushing was 636 tons, mostly of ore that
would not pay dividends when treated separately, but a
few tons of the rich shoot added brought the yield up to
the average—considerably over an ounce to the ton. And
this elasticity and the control exercised over the yields
were always'accentuated at Christmastide, when, with
the usual number of tons crushed, Keam generally
trebled the average yield of gold for the “Christmas
cake.” “ What would the rich vein crushed by itself give
a ton?” was once asked of an underground employ'd.
“Well, aa wuddn’t like to say, for fear thee’d
think ad’d be tellin’ ee lies?” was the reply, and no one
could answer the question with certainty, for the ore was
always mixed with the poorer stuff. The remark is
often made, “ It’s easy to manage a mine that is yielding
plenty of gold—yet, within a mile radius of this grand
mine are scores of others that in their halcyon days
turned out gold just as prodigally as the Garden Gully,
and in which there are untold lons of ore in sight, winch
will not pay to work. There may be rich shoots of gold
in them, but it won’t pay to seek them. The Hustler s
mines may be instanced, as well as many on the New
Chum and Garden Gully lines. Ream’s reign stands as a
model for all mining managers.

Dear Bulletin,—Re bank-profits out of lost
notes &0., a German at one time trading at a Murray
bend had much business every s-ason with a big camp of
blacks. Trading was mostly by way of barter: the blacks
brought skins, &0., and accepted rations, rum, tobacco,
blankets, &c., &c. Any balance due to a nig. was always
paid by cheque. Business over, the German would start
things with a couple of bolt les of rum, and leave. Inside
a few hours Tophet would reign. There was always a fire !
Out of over 100 cheques every season not a-third ever
turned up at the bank to the trader s debit. \\ est-
ward Ho. *
# * *
Rock-breakers and self-feeders are all very well
before batteries under certain conditions, but as a rule
they' are not recommended, and while all automatic
machinery works without judgment self-feeders will not
be extensively used. We have often seen them put in,
and then pitched on the scrap-heap. Experience has
shown that in nine cases out of ten the cost of hand-feed-
ing'is more than repaid by Ihe increased capacity. The
only qualification required by a battery-feeder is good
judgment, and of even that there is very little in N.S.W.
# * *
Directors in N.S.W. too often regard the
mining manager’s report as “copy” that requires sub-
editing. Generally it is only the good reports that are
published in N.S.W.
# # *
It is of no use Southern miners rushing to the
Coen and the Batavia. The prospects are shadowy, the
climate very bad, the country absolutely unsettled, and
the niggers aggressive.
It is rumoured, should the salaries of the
Queensland puisne judges be not increased, Justice
Real will leave the Bench and resume bar practice.
The Northumberland miners seem inclined
to mete out to Ninny Melville the treatment he
once accorded Parkes— i.e., the refusal of a hearing.
A Lithgow (N.S.W.) clergyman relates
that a woman who gave birth to twins in the
Jubilee year christened them Ju and Bilee re-
spectively.
A case for Lombroso. A healthy and
vigorous N.S.W. miner with an intelligent wife
has a large family in which the boys are all idiots,
while the girls have exceptional mental ability.
The man who arrived in Australia with
only “ sixpence in his pocket ” is getting as mono-
tonous as the man who, like John Pope, of Fanner
and Cos., dates his rise to glory from the picking
up of a pin. Alderman Meeks, of Sydney, is the
latest sixpenny hero. ...
Apathy seems to be the curse of the
N.S.VV. Labour-party. Deducting half-a-dozen
men of active minds, the balance are incapable of
prolonged mental effort, and, collapsing under the
strain, they leave the struggle with the National
Association to the born fighters, and fade away
into the domino rooms, or get behind a magazine
to race downstairs when the division bell rings. If
every member of the crowd, about 30, would speak
half an hour in straight terms every time a plank
was in danger the Government would be
“squeezed” oftener.

Thought and Prayer.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Were all the Sorrow floating on the breath
Churned into Charity by Life Sincere,
Hunger and Poverty might smile at Death,
And heavenly joys be entered into Here.
How long, O God, must Misery await
Vengeance on those who shut the Gates of Life !
Is Thy voice shouting from the walls —Too Late?
End all, 0 God, or end this hopeless strife !
Open the Gates, 0 God, open them wide,
Open men’s hearts—be on the Wretched’s side !
Sept. 24th, 1892.
TAS. BUSHMAN.
“Faustina,” in Sydney D.T., quotes a
few lines from Browning, that most people think
fearfully naughty.—
The sin I impute to each frustrate ghost
Is—the unlit lamp and the ungirt loin.
But it’s only the Browningesque way of referring
to the parable of “ The Foolish Virgins.”
S.M. Herald takes care to mention that
two banks have taken up a Melbourne Corpora-
tion loan “in equal moieties at par.” What is
wanted now is for two banks to take something
up in “unequal halves.” “Yes,” once said
Adelaide’s then Mayor (now Sir), E. T. Smith,
“ the Council pays one moiety, the Government
pays one moiety, and the insurance companies
pay the other moiety.” So moiety it be.
Dear Bulletin, —A simple remedy for
malarial fever may be of service to many of your
Gulf readers who do not reside near a chemist’s
shop :—Dig down on the tap-root of the quinine
bush, strip off bark, and boil slowly in billy of
water for half-an-hour, then strain liquor off into
brandy-bottle, add a couple of packets of salts,
and take a wine-glass full before meals. In the
absence of any other medicine this has been found
most effective.—Yours, Baktrax,
The history of the good deeds of Sir
Henry Parkes may be written in sand, but he has
taken steps to ensure some of his evil actions
teing embodied in stone —soft sandstone, fortu-
nately. The artist—we mean the carver—who
sculped - that is. to say, committed—the decora-
tions of the Sydney Post-office facade was further
commissioned to prepare statues to fill the niches
in the new Sydney Lands-office. Two of these,
namely alleged representations of Wentworth and
Sir John Robertson, have been fixed in their
places, and although they provide amusement to
the daily passers by it is nothing less than a
scandal that they should be allowed to remain
where they are. It is bad enough to have to pay
for such rubbish, but it is infinitely worse that the
expensive public buildings of an important city
should be permanently disfigured by such disgrace-
ful exhibitions.
lii most of the rural English parishes,
every inhabitant is required to pay a fee —varying
from 3s. 4d. to 10s.—to the Anglican'parson for
the privilege of dying; that is to say, the shepherd
drops on his surviving relatives for the money.
Then there is another fee for the right to be
buried. Deceased has to buy his grave and to pay
the reverend for the right to use it. There
is an extra fee on top of this for the privilege
of being interred in consecrated ground ;
in other words, deceased has to buy a piece
of consecration at so much per lb. There is
a fee for reading the burial-service; a special fee
of .£lO for the privilege of being buried in a brick
grave; a fee of ,£1 Is. for the privilege of using a
leaden coffin ; an extra fee for the right to erect a
monument, and some more fee for permission to
put a railing around the grave. And lastly, by
way of getting there like a centipede, with a
hundred feet at once, the gospel-grinder hires out
the graveyard for grazing purposes. Nobody can
make so much out of a corpse as the British
Established parson.

Sam. stood six foot three in each stocking,
And drank like a fish all the day ;
His passion for liquor was shocking,
Till Death came and biffed him away.
They knew there was only one pleasure
That bibulous Sammy held dear—
So, carefully taking his measure,
They brought him a very long bier.

Auckland Star, May 7, 1892.
MOTHER ALBERT’S REMEDIES.
In these days of patent medicines it requires
that certificates of excellence should be signed by
the names of persons of standing in the com-
munity. The herbal remedies put up by the Rev.
Mother Mary Joseph Aubert, of Jerusalem,
Wanganui River, have already been highly spoken
of, and elsewhere will be seen an advertisement
which publishes in full the testimony of such
eminent personages as Lord Onslow, Bishop
Grimes, of Christchurch, Bishop Redwood, of
Wellington, and, passing from the pulpit to the
stage, Mr. Walter Bentley. The latter acknow-
ledges the wonderful result to his voice from using
“Marupa.” Another preparation designated
“Karana ” is said to be quite equal to combating
most of the ills of life, and restores a broken-down
constitution. It is a vegetable tonic, pleasant to
take, and by no means hurtful in its action.
These valuable medicines are now being put up by
Messrs Kempthorne, Prosser and Co.
Takaka News, June 23, 1892,
MOTHER MARY JOSEPH ALBERT’S
REMEDIES.
The efficacy of the New Zealand herbal remedies
prepared by the Maoris is too well known by all
old settlers to need comment, and the wonderful
medicinal properties of several native shrubs have
been testified to by many in our midst. The
experience of a long life spent among the natives
in the North Island has induced a'lady belonging
to a Religious Order of the Catholic Church to
make known to the public the several prepara-
tions, the marvellous effects of which she has
observed during her sojourn amongst the natives.
The testimonials which will be found in another
column, are from those who, having derived great
benefit from the remedies, desire to testify to their
efficacy. When we find a Governor, a Bishop,
and a distinguished actor, unite in recommending
these preparations, it may be safely assumed that
it is because they deserve it. Full particulars of
the recently-established agency for this district
will be found elsewhere.
Evening Post, June 21, 1892.
From private letters received from Mr. Orlando
R. Kempthorne, who, as we stated some few
weeks ago, was proceeding to Sydney and Mel-
bourne to introduce the Aubert remedies there,
we leArn that he has been most successful in
gettirlg the bulk goods that he took to Sydney put
up smartly, and disposed of in a very short time.
He has ordered more to be forwarded at once, as
be has none left to take to Melbourne. He states
that he has every reason to hope that his tour will
prove a gigantic success. The New South Wales
papers congratulate Mr. Kempthorne on having
introduced into their colony a medicine that has
become so widely known and so warmly appre-
ciated in New Zealand.
Wairarapa Daily, June 6, 1892,
Lnsqlicited testimony still continues to pour in
regarding the wonderful efficacy of Mother Mary
Joseph Aubert’s remedies. The marvellous cures
wrought upon persons well nigh at death’s door*
have caused a profound sensation, and on many
thousand tongues are words of gratitude and
blessing for the good lady who has devoted hef
life to the alleviation in some measure of the
world’s suffering.
Otago Daily Times, June 28, 1892.
Mr. O. R. Kempthorne, of the New Zealand
Drug Company, recently visited Sydney to in-
troduce there the Rev. Mother Mary J. Aubert’s
remedies, made entirely from New Zealand herbs
and plants, which have met with so much favour
in this colony. He was most successful, the fame
of the curative properties of the remedies having
preceded their arrival, and it is satisfactory to
find that a new item has been added to our list of
exports, which it is expected will soon make its
way into the old country, where the remedies
have one warm friend in Lord Onslow.
New Zealand Standard, June 17, 1892.
The Aubert Remedies.—A new advertisement
from Messrs Kempthorne, Prosser and Co., in
reference to the above, appears in another column.
These remedies are daily becoming more famous
for their efficacy in the treatment of all sorts of
ailments, so much so that increased facilities have
had to be provided by the manufacturer for their
production. Mr. George Foster is the local agent.
Hawkes Bay Herald, July 2, 1892.
Mr. Kempthorne, of the firm of Kempthorne,
and Prosser, has lately been on a visit to Sydney
in connection with Mother Mary Joseph Aubert’s
remedies, and it appears that he has had wonder-
ful success there, the remedies having come into
great favour in New South Wales.
A Maoriland functionary, who owns one
ten-pound share in a Christchurch paper, is said
to have paid <£looo for it just before last election.
A Parramatta (N.S.W.) firm has invented
a “flying-fox scarer ” —a machine to be fixed up
among fruit-trees to scare off the flying-fox pest.
It is a revolving bull’s-eye lantern that is wound
up overnight and keeps going till morning, with
the cheerful accompaniment of banging a gong
“ with a noise as loud as the braying of a donkey,”
every two minutes.

Melbourne Public Library authorities are
very exigent of public morals. They have a
splendid edition of Voltaire, in 70 vols., but “La
Pucelle ” is withdrawn from the shelves, and even
Hawthorne’s “ Scarlet Letter ” has met with the
same expurgation from the collected edition of his
works.
At a little drought-stricken W.Q. town-
ship there is but one pub., kept by a woman whose
Imsband manages a big station close by. Anybody
with a swollen red nose, pimpled countenance,
and two shaky hands can get a job at that station,
but unless a man has the indubitable signs of hard
and continuous drinking written on his counten-
ance he has no show. Almost all the cheques that
are paid on the station are drunk at the pub., and
everybody who has drunk a big cheque at the pub.
is sure of a job at the station, so a ted-nosed pro-
cession wanders back and forward between the
two places without ceasing, and great are the
profits that ensue.
Twas in the early days, and the station
was young. A neighbouring super, rode up as the
boss came out of the hut with a bucket, knives
and a rifle. Super. : “Good day! Going to
kill?’ Boss: “Good day! Yes.” Super.;
“ I’ll hobble my horse and lend you a hand.”
Boss: “All right.” Super, follows down to the
yard and sees boss waiting for a roan bullock to
look at him. Super. ; “Which are you going to
shoot?” Boss: “The roan.” Super.: “That be
d—d for a yarn ! He’s mine.” “ Boss : “Yours ?”
Super.: “Yes, look at his brand.” Boss: “I
really beg your pardon, I thought it was Jobson’s ”
—his next-door neighbour.
A Reefton (M.L.) correspondent writes; —
Just a few lines to know if you could give me any
information concerning the name of the young man who
met his death travelling with a team in the N.S.W.
western district. I read the account given in your paper,
dated Aug. 27th, and much desire to know the town it
happened in. A young friend wrote, two months ago,
telling me he was coming with a team to Sydney, there-
fore I am very anxious.
The par in question concerned a young man, name
unknown, who was found with both legs crushed
beneath a broken-down dray, on a western road,
and died during the process of atnputatiom The
Bulletin cannot, at the present moment, locate
the occurrence. Possibly sotne Western header cat!
furnish our correspondent with names and exact
date.

CONSULTATION FREE BY LETTER OR PERSONAL VISIT.
Dr. Kelly’s reputation is so thoroughly established that it will only oe necessary for him to intimate
that he is practising as a Specialist, to be appreciated as a benefactor to suffering mankind. As the
greater portion of the New South Wales public is aware, Dr. Kelly is a Legally Qualified and Registered
Physician, and has by far the largest practice in the colonies. Many persons at a distance, however, are
Unaware of his great success, as, hitherto, he has refrained from advertising. Hence, to widen the
sphere of his usefulness, he has determined, by means of advertising, to make himself known in remote
districts, where his reputation may not yet have travelled. As the question may arise, why does so
eminent a Physician as Dr. Kelly find it necessary to advertise, he may reply to one and all as
follows: —That the Physician who has the power to cure, and remains silent, is guilty of an un-
pardonable sin of omission ; for if there is a person on earth to be pitied, it is he who is obliged to
associate with his fellow beings while his mind is constantly harassed and tortured by the consciousness
of his past folly.

The Bookmaker’s Father.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
A tear stood on the nether lid of Barney Boko’s
eye;
He spake a string of vulgar words that terminate
in “ y ” ;
His frame with strange emotion shook, he scratched
his level head—
“ If I’m not telling Bible truth,” quoth Barney,
“ strike me dead !
“ I left my ’ome in sixty-nine, and come out here’
I did,
I wasn’t then a something man, nor yet a some-
thing kid,
But in between the something two, a bland, old-
fashioned youth,
What cut his native land before he cut his wisdom
tooth.
“ I didn’t ask my father for his blessing when I
went —
We ’ad a row and didn’t waste no bloomin’ senti-
ment ;
I left him in his korfee-shop, the place where I
was born,
And mother couldn’t kiss me, ’cos, poor woman !
she was gorn.
“ ‘Get out o’ this, ungrateful swine !” I heard the
old ’un say,
As, lumbered with my carpet-bag, I tore myself
away;
‘Don’t let me see your face no more.’ Sez I,
‘You needn’t fear ’
A h’egg was father’s last remark to fall upon my
ear.
“ And so I took my passage in the steerage, with
the stuff
I’d been and scraped together—it was barely just
enough ;
My word ! it was a voyidge ; for a fortnight I was
sick —
And I landed here in Melbourne with a shilling in
my ‘ kick. ’
“ Life wasn’t beer ah’ skittles just at first; I had
to shift
For a year on next to nothing ’fore I ever got a
lift ;
Then I knocked about at racing (which I tumbled
was the game)
Till I started with a satchel, and a ’undred to my
name.
“ It was easy graft in them times ; one ’ad only got
to lay
Harf the something price the public want a-tempt-
ing with to-day;
When ’Ari-co£, Lord love ’im ! won the Cup I felt
a prince—
That night I bought a buggy, and I’ve drove one
ever since.
“ Then I settled down and married, and, let me
win or lose,
I always had the sense to play for safety with the
‘ booze.’
Good fortune spoils some blokes, but ever since I
first began
To hold the stuff, I’ve tried to be the something
gentleman.
“ And what about my father? Well, I’d written
him a line,
But never got no letter in acknowledgment of
mine;
And Mossy, when in Ingland, couldn’t rout ’im
out, or drop
On anyone who’d seen ’im, for he’d left thekorfee-
shop.
“ A father is a father, and, with children of my
own,
I orfen thought about ’im when I found myself
alone.
And wished we’d parted friendly, as a son an’
father should —
For I ain’t a something image, with a blanky soul
of ivood.
“ ’S’help me Bob! I’m thankful that we met
again at last.
He come upon me sudden, like a mem’ry of the
past.
The way in which it ’appened was the rummest
kind of chance—
Some writer ought to take it for the plot of some
romance.
“Two months ago, at Randwick, a thin, white-
’eaded chap
Comes up as I was bettin’ on the Final Handicap,
And sez—‘What price The Ripper?’ in a voice
quite sorft and sad—
I was just about to lay ’im, when I sor it was my
dad.
“lories, ‘Wot! don’t yer know me—don’t yer
know yer blanky son ?’
’E didn't, he was dazed like, and he wanted ten
to one.
All at once he shouted ‘ Barney !’ and began to
shake an’ sniff,
Well, I wouldn’t ’ ave my father, so I whispered,
‘ Ripper’s stiff.’
“ There was lots of fellers round us, so I looked in
father’s face,
And said, ‘ Just wait a minit, till we’ve polished
orf this race;
But if of that there sov-er-eign you’re anxious to
get rid,
The Gift is on the job —you ’ave a ‘pony’, to a
‘ quid.’
“He stood aside and waited while I did a Ijt of
biz.,
Then we walked away together, with my’and a-
gripping his,
To see the ’orses finish, as we stood against the
rail—
‘Good Gord ! ’ sez I to father, ‘you are orful
something pale!’
“ The Gift come in a length ahead, and father
give a cry—
A something shriek that rent the air and split the
blanky sky;
He fell down flat upon the grass, and murmured,
‘ Bless you, boy ! ’
Before he drew one garsplng breath, and died of
dashy joy.
“ At first the shock seemed bloomin’ rough and
sent me nearly wild,
But soon it cheered me up to think we’d both been
reconciled.
A father’s blessin’ —fools may laugh, but men don’t
mind their sneers—
Is something, when you haven’t met for two and
twenty years.
“ I’d acted Zionest, that’s the point, and didn’t try
to best
The poor old chap, who might have dropped his
sugar, like the rest.
But if I’d let him back a horse I knew was cronk
and lame,
Why, he’d ’ave dammed me up and down, and
perished just the same.
“The doctor sent him out, I found, because his
’ealth was bad;
His ’eart was weak, and arf a lung was all the old
’un ’ad.
He’d fainted orf the night before while playin’
penny Nap.,
His race was finished when they’d run the Final
Handicap.
“ A second wife she collared all the stuff he ’ad to
leave.
And ’urried back to Ingland, larfin’, maybe, in
her sleeve.
For I paid to get 'im buried, and I bought a stone,
that’s more,
With the six-and-twenty sov’reigns that he didn’t
live to drore.”
A tear dropped from the nether lid of Barney’s
bloodshot eye,
He made a noise that sounded like an alligator’s
sigh.
And solemnly he raised his hat from off his level
head—
“lf I’m not telling Bible truth,” quoth Barney,
“ strike me dead !”
EDMUND FISHER.
Melbourne.
* Written on the night of Glenloth’s Cup.
The X.Y.Z. Cos. own some half-dozen
stations in Southern Queensland. A vacancy
occurring in the management of Crow Creek,
young M'Gingan rode over to Humpabluey and
applied to Grindem, tire general manager, for the
billet. “Well, Mr. M'Gingan,” said Grindem,
“I intend cutting down all my managers’
salaries. The Co.’s affairs have been conducted
on too expensive a scale. In future, managers
will have to do their own clerical work, and their
book-keeper’s services may be dispensed with.
Work which has been paid for at the rate of .£SOO
a year will have to be done at ,£200.” To whom
M'Gingan, smilingly: “Then the auditors will
overhaul the station-books, find the manager’s
work has been done quite as satisfactorily as in
the old days of high salaries ; and an immediate
sequel will be a reduction in the general manager’s
salary. Work, for which he is paid £IIOOO a year,
now may be done just as well for <£soo. What
do you think, Mr. Grindem?” You could have
heard an elephant drop, and then Grindem said :
“Have a whisky, M'Gingan.” MGingan has
the management of Crow Creek, and all the
salaries are the same as heretofore.
Says Miss Frances Holden in a contem-
porary : —
Cancer is due—to mention some salient and suggestive
particulars—to tough, ill-cooked meats; waxy, ill-cooked
potatoes ; over-drawn, semi-poisonous tea ; greasy, fried
foods ; hot soda cakes, sodden or otherwise ; new bread of
any sort, and other such-like abominations.
Miss Holden evidently knows more than anyone
else in the world about cancer.
Col. Hobbs (S.M. Herald) writes deny-
ing the story of a recent correspondent that the
thighbones of a horse had been palmed off upon
him as the remains of Cunabo-Gunnerah, the
aboriginal terror of the Namoi, which he scouts as
something not calculated to deceive a 10-year-old
child. Says the Colonel: “The bone of a two-
legged ass might raise complications, as the
difference between the bone-structure of an animal
having verticle (sic!) and one having horizontal
(why not “horizontle,” Colonel?) movement would
not be so readily seen.” The Colonel says that
Dr. Haynes exhibited the whole of the aborigine’s
bones, with the exception of a few ribs, one foot
and a couple of joints of the vertebrae; and that
Dr. Haynes and he reconstructed the whole
skeleton in the former’s surgery. The Colonel
claims to have verified his “notes” among the
blacks themselves. Further, the Colonel denies
having recently lectured in Cooktown, N.Q.,
advocating slavery, saying:—“I delivered a
charity ‘talk,’ and defended the Southern slave-
owners from the charge of general cruelty, but
stated that they would not have the slaves again.
I did not even justify slavery.”

BLOOD.
Pure blood is a life-long delight. It is health—real good health and
Strength—free from pimples, spots, sores and aches.
Have pure blood by taking Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets internally, and use
a really good modern soap —Frazer’s Sulphur Soap—externally, and you
ensure a clear skin and a good complexion.
It is a mistake to suppose that a good skin or a clear complexion can be
secured by Soap alone. It cannot. A pure Soap—Frazer’s Sulphur Soap
is an invaluable aid to personal beauty, but the first essential is pure blood.
This must never be forgotten. If we can’t have the blood quite pure, then
we must get it as pure as we can.
A man wants a clear skin and a wholesome complexion, in profession or
business. Else if he be blotchy, pimply, or disfigured, other men fight shy
of dealing with him. Let the man take Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets internally
and use Frazer’s Sulphur Soap externally.
A woman needs a good complexion else her happiness is decreased, and
her charm to the other sex much lessened. Let the woman take Frazer’s
Sulphur Tablets internally and use Frazer’s Sulphur Soap externally.
A child for its health’s sake needs a rosy skin, free from eruptive
blemish, and a blood free from the taint of fever and infectious germs. Let
the child take Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets internally and use Frazer’s Sulphur
Soap externally.
Frazer’s'Sulphur Tablets used internally ensures good blood—pure blood
that holds disease at bay and gives health.
Frazer’s Sulphur Soap renders the skin soft, supple and smooth, neither
drying the oils of the skin, nor shrivelling the nails, nor splitting the hairs.
It is a milled soap, contains extra balm, and is free from excess of alkali or
fat. It is simple and truly the best of all Soaps, and contains Oil of
Eucalyptus.
Frazer’s Sulphur Tablets are put up in 1/6 packets (post free 1/8 );
Frazer’s Sulphur Soap, in Green Boxes, price 9d. (Post free, lid.) Three
packets, post free, 2/6.
Frazer’s productions are sold by, or may be ordered of, all chemists and
medicine vendors at home or in any part of the world. Sole Proprietors,
Frazer’s Tablets, Ltd., 11, Ludgate Square, London, E.C.

Magdalen,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
This that they do is my reward for sin,
Eor sin and shame have ever been akin
And dwell together in the heart of me,
Where joys and sorrows turned to curses be,
Where never loving kindness shall win in.
For, while I stand despised, grand dames sweep
by,
Who sold themselves for gold to slavery,
To men for whom not even a little love
They had ; yet virtue is reward thereof—
The virtue of the churches, not the sky !
In years agone I fell for love of one—
Sweet love it was that loosed my virgin zone;
He left me alter, bearing in my womb
The sign of shame, the mark of my own doom ?
A harlot now, what else could I have done ?
They were but harlots from the first, wild sold
Their ivory bodies for the lust of gold
To men whd bought them for the hist of flesh ; ■
Clear-eyed, they sought by choice the devil's
mesh;
This is truth only that I now have told !
Yet do they scorn me, who with me are kin.
Although between us is a wall so thin
That none may see material thereof;
Church creeds applaud them as at me they scoff;
And point me orit as one deep-srink in siri.
Yet where!ii diffet lin siti from the§o
Who lift the lip of sedtn and stiff the knees ?
Seeing tile hollow-eyed, -With Harlot cheek,
Arid; seeing, thank In tones that are not meek;
The hatd-btdWed Gdd Of all the PhatiSebs.
Arid thus they say—“ C Lord, vVd ate I'hy saints;
And serVe the letter of Thy law’s restraints
In holiness; We ate not such as these
Who sell their bodies unto lust for fees—
Hath riot church-Wedding held Us from such
taints ?”
And down itt hell, hearing, the devils laugh
And tell the fate of them that loved the Calf
Of Gold itt Israel, and the Pharisee
Who said, “ Not as this publican I be !”
Hell knows that prayer and the reward thereof !
There was a White Christ once, with heart of
dove,
With lovingkindness all men else above,
Through Whose brown eyes in pity looked His
soul
On them that strove in Vain and failed the goal ;
But where is now the Christ of Hope and Love ?
Down from the altars are His statues rolled,
The Church has east out pity from its fold,
And lovingkindness thrown from out its door,
For Christ it knows and heeds not any more,
But has gone back unto the Calf of Gold !

Serial stories are forbidden in Turkey, and
the press censor promptly stops the issue of any
paper containing a tale “to be continued in our
next.” It is held that the mental strain and
anxiety of waiting for the next instalment pf the
yarn unsettle the public mind, and thereby sow.
the seeds of revolution, and that the only way to
keep the nation calm and contented is to have
all stories complete in one issue. The Bulletin
has long recognised this.
Of the ex-Empress Eugenie, Sir Richard
Wallace wrote :
She had paroxysms of mawkish philanthropy, as when
she insisted that the prostitute inmates of Saint Lazare
should have dessert after their dinner. The same woman
would, at the end of a hunt, deliberately jump off her
horse, plunge a gleaming knife into the throat of a pant*
ing stag, and revel in the sight of blood. Many who saw
her do this argued that, in the hour of danger she would
as boldly face the enemies of herself and her dynasty. The
event proved that they were Utterly mistaken. She slunk
away at the supreme hour; while the Princess Clotilde,
whom she had presumed to teach the manners of a court,
left Paris like a princess, in an open landau preceded by
an out-rider.
The Arts and Crafts Exhibition, now being
held in Sydney, under the auspices of the local
Architectural Association, deserves recognition,
if only because the show is the first of its kind in
Australasia, The idea of holding these exhibitions
in England originated with Walter Crane, who
stands in the front rank of decorative artists,
using the term in its widest possible sense, and
who is also an ardent socialist, working hand and
glove with William Morris, the poet, in assisting
the cause of Labour. The Sydney experiment
has, of course, a narrower scope than its prototype,
but it, nevertheless, carries with it an important
lesson. One has only to look at the admirable
metalwork on view here, and the wrought-iron
gates for the Town Hall enclosure (the latter not
only made in New South Wales but also produced
from local ore).
A London writer ;
The average English husband tires of his wife, but so he
would of a harem if he had one. Man never is, but always
to be blest. Wisdom is a rare quality in this world, but
still rarer is that of being content with one’s lot. I have
a strong conviction that the average English husband gets
at least the wife he deserves, and when he whines about
the superiority of the French woman or the German he is
like the musician who complains of his violin. Instrument
for instrument, the Englishwoman is, I have no doubt,
equal to the daughter of any other country. It is for the
husband to get out of her what tune he can, and the
result will probably depend in a greater degree than he
suspects upon his own powers as a virtuoso. It
has been remarked in this connection that the
man who finds in his wife a good cook or a good
housekeeper, and nothing more, will be very much obliged
to her, but will go elsewhere for his romance. But what
is his romance? I should like a definition of romance as
it is understood by the suburban classes. Consider the
daily round of life on a small income, and how little
opportunity there is on the one side or the other for
romance to come in ! The husband, having been in the
city all day, comes home to an evening meal, and after a
pint of beer or other soporific falls asleep over the evening
paper. The wife, for her part, has had a hard dav, too,
and, having got the children to bed, is glad to spend an
hour with the work-basket. If in such a household the
wife has fathomed the mysteries of plain roast and boiled,
and knows how to handle a baby, “ Married Man” ought
to be confoundedly well satisfied. He gets out of marriage
as much as, or more than, he brings to it.
